RATING: 5.5
Interesting use of dice, but not all that fun gameplay. I get excited as my turn approaches, but the actual turns are dissapointing, as you don't usually roll as awesome as you are hoping for. The game is also very unforgiving, keeping players down who get off to a bad start, while letting players who score points ditch crappy dice so their dice pool gets even better. The dragon that is immune to level 1 and 2 monsters is rediculous and makes almost all other monsters useless, and portals just aren't very fun (a portal! let's see what's inside! *rolls* manna! Oh, I meant Quiddity!).

Also, WAY too many stupid Q words that really just shouldn't be there.

RATING: 5.5
The randomness of rolling the dice, added to the randomness of which dice are drawn from your bag, make this game simply too... random.  I don't find the dice included in the game particularly well-crafted. They're small and some symbols (particularly the numbers) are difficult to read, especially from across the table.  And there just isn't enough to this game to make it interesting.

+Unique twist on deck building games.

-Excessive randomness (random dice draw + dice rolling) makes for uneven play.
-Dice are small and difficult to read.
-Even thinner theme than Dominion.

RATING: 5

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Almost like Dominion, but with dice. The only problem is the balance issues. Dominion is fairly balanced because there's less luck involved and your card powers are fixed. There's a lot more luck in Quarriors (since your monster could just give you Quiddity instead of being an actual monster) and a lot of the monsters are unbalanced (i.e. they are practically guaranteed to win just due to their defense). Still, those that like randomness would like this. At least it has a bit more theme than Dominion...
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
The base game system itself is terrible, and the "expert" rules fix some of its inherent flaws by jettisoning the idea of building a dice bag over time--which is the most fun part of this kind of game!  Strangely enough, though, I still kind of like Quarriors.  Even if the rules are bad, buying and rolling tons of cool-looking custom dice is just fun.  This is a good game to play with people who don't want something too think-y because they don't have to think and can have a reasonable chance of winning anyway.

[2-4] Need to try with the advanced variant:
1) Base 2 die buys per turn instead of 1
2) Can't buy 2 of the same die on a turn 
3) No exhausted creature game end
-) You can only cull the creature you score and you have the option of keeping the creature, not scoring it, and culling another used die

RATING: 7
Hmm...It's like [b]Dominion[/b] in a couple of ways, but it adds a whole new dimension.  That dimension is an extra layer of randomness from the dice.  You may think you're buying a cool power, but it may only show up a fraction of the time.

The playing time is enjoyably short, but I think the randomness works against that: a couple of turns of bad rolls seems to really hinder you.

I think there's real potential here, but I have to play it more to adjust to the limitations of the game.

Update:  Just played with 2.0 rules.  It changes the dynamic of the game so it feels (and flows) different, but it just doesn't fix the game.  I still really want to like this.

RATING: 5.5
Beautiful cards and fantastic dice. The theme of the game is great, components are top notch.

Game play wise is average as there are nothing more that dice collection, rolling them and doing your best to get the most out of what the dice allows you to do. So in a sense, the dice plays you, you don't play the game......

After the 50th dice rolled.....boredom sets in. Not as bad as dominion.....controls so many minions.....but limited in use. Nothing to battle, besides just 'besting' the single enemy.....no dungeon crawl, no heavy conflicts, no epic battles. Just roll the dice, collect 'price' if surpass.....

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
I like dice games, but I also like control. Quarriors suffers from too much luck: luck of the draw + luck of the roll. There are very few ways to mitigate bad rolls (or bad draws) in this game, so basically, if you roll well at the beginning of a game, you should win. Runaway leader problems and too much randomness really damper the fun factor. However, there are optional "advanced" rules (from Quarmageddon) that help solve this problem. Quarriors scores points for the beauty of its bits and the originality of its packaging (and branding in general). While not a masterpiece, it is a light and fun game.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 6.5
Very light, very fast take on the deckbuilding game, but I'm becoming convinced that the correct play is virtually always to buy the biggest monster available, since these are simultaneously the most damaging, the hardest to remove, and score enough points to win the game if they survive only a few times in a 4-player game, making it just a game about racing to be the first one to play a dragon.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Deckbuilding games and dice games both tend to get a cool reception from me.  So what chance a dice-building game?  Against the odds, I quite enjoyed Quarriors.  

Re-rolling is sensibly kept in check to deliver quick turns and a quick game overall.  There are, of course, different powered monsters and spells to pick up and try to combine; although in this I found Quarriors much less sophisticated than Dominion.  There is also player interaction, with monsters fighting and spells going off.  In short, I found it kind of fun, if not deep.

Quarriors is nicely packaged and those dice are colourful.  Problems came from reading the itty figures on them and from the need to read card text across the table.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Dice-building battling game.  It's fun building your dice pool, but there is too much luck to rate higher.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Quarriors! is a dice building game similar to how Dominion is a deck building game (and so far I think Quarriors! is the better game).

It is easy to learn, fast to play, has good components and artwork, and has substantial variability for replay value.  Though it is a dice-fest there is strategic depth to the game.

Overall I think it fits in well as a lighter game or as 30 min filler game.

RATING: 2
Basically a dice-version of Dominion.  With lots of luck.  In fact, it's almost all luck, with almost no strategic decisions to make.  You roll the dice, and either summon the only creature you have, or buy the only creature you can afford that is worth buying.  And there will be a runaway leader.  Would be improved if there were multiple paths to victory, so players could still compete even if they get a step behind the mad rush to buy the one creature that is any good in the match. This game would be great with kids.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
The only deck (dice) building game other than Dominion where I devour every expansion like croissants.  I love the feel of the dice, I love the variety from game to game.  Where I believe this game fails a little is in the complexity actually.  Once the dice are pooled in the wilds before the game actually starts, there is a lot of card ability reading and creature stat comparing that slows down what should be a fun casual dice rolling game.  Then again, if it were simplified maybe it wouldn't have the staying power and draw that it does.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Verdict: neat.  A "dice-building" game a la Dominion with tiny card-specific dice rather than cards or chips.  The dice have special symbols on them with meaning denoted on the cards, so there can be wildly different versions of dice simply by changing out a single card.  I really like that system.  As for the game, and the effects of the cards I played, it made me yearn for Dominion's relative simplicity and elegance.  Not a fan of the non-standard box, or the lack of turn order adjustments.  I think this game is on the right path in evolving the genre, and look forward to further variants on the theme.  I could easily see looking back on this as a missing link for builders (unlike, say, Ascension or Puzzle Strike).
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Fun game.  I worry that some of the creatures are overpowered - it seemed unlikely that anything could take out the Quake Dragon in my one game.  I need more plays, but definitely a good time.

SECOND PLAY: I think the three-player game is better than the four-player game.  It doesn't seem as short.  I look forward to trying it with two.

THIRD PLAY: Nope.  Not working for me.  Most DBGs rely on randomness with the card draw, but this adds a die roll to that randomness.  The dice are really cool, but the gameplay is just too clunky and not very fun to me.

RATING: 6
From my point of view, this is a dice version of Thunderstone, and ranks about the same, but as a quicker lighter variant.

The main advantage of Quarriors over Thunderstone is the quicker setup and cleanup.  The main disadvantage is the shallower story a game session tells.

Luck is in fact really no different.  The illusion is that Thunderstone is lower luck, but it is not.  They are equivalent.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 2
Really fun game, as long as you don't mind the luck factor being quite strong. I've only played the base game once at this point, because the game pieces were extremely bad right out of the box and they need to be replaced.

The quality of the dice (both the inking and the molding) and of the cards in my particular set is horrendous.  The condition of the contents looked more like a game bought at a thrift store, rather than brand new in shrink wrap (and for $60, I might add). 

I also bought the expansion:  Quarriors: Rise of the Demons, which was in perfect condition except for missing paint/ink on one die.  So it's not as though the quality can't be achieved, just that inferior product is being made and shipped.

After multiple emails to the publisher I have yet to receive a response, and their replacement system only allows five replacements of dice or cards (and this is only one of each type, whereas I need a total of 21 dice and an entirely new deck of cards--every card in the set was badly creased). 

So for game play based on one play, I'd give it a solid 9.  However, for quality of product and customer support, I drop that to a 1.

This is a shame, as I really wanted to like this game, but every single time I take it down from the shelf I end up putting it back, because it irks me all over again that Wiz Kids has such poor regard for their customers.  Just looking at the condition of the cards will ruin any enjoyment I might have from playing the game. 

If I ever hear back from Wiz Kids, maybe I'll adjust my rating.

2/28/13: Adjusted rating to a 2, as I finally received a response from customer support.

3/13/13: Sticking with a 2, as Wiz Kids will not replace the damaged cards and will only replace 5 of the dice unless the rep can get "special permission".  I deem this too much trouble to go through to get what I paid for in the first place, so I'm chalking this one up as a loss of $60 for me, and a loss of a customer for them.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5.5
The new take on deckbuilding is a huge commercial success, I hear. I suspect this is mainly due to the fact that, for a MSRP of 50 EUR, one gets 130 custom dice (along with many cards - more than one per type of die - and some other stuff). That is very impressive, even for non-geeks!
The game itself is a pretty decent filler, of course very luck driven, but funny enough to be enjoyed even by the eurogamer in me. The tactile sensation of putting a hand in a bag full of dice is not a thing that a true geek can dismiss so easily...
With more than two players the game gets slightly better. My rating is a 4 with two, and 5 with three or four players.

Update may 7th, 2012: I found a couple of variants on BGG forums (Advanced Capture Rule, The Totally Expert Rule of Culling), that will be officially released with the Quarmageddon expansion. Those variants definitely improve the game, mitigate luck and add some more strategy to the game. With them, the rating of Quarriors! raises to a full 5.5.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
Note: After first few plays, we have been playing with the 2 buy per turn variant.

Got the game this week, and have already played over thirty times. Although there is a good amount of randomness (the winner is not always the person who played the best) there is more meaningful strategy here than in Dominion. I usually feel that once you have reached a certain level of mastery of Dominion moves become fairly obvious, and there is no level of interaction. In this game there are meaningful decisions every turn, there is more interaction, and turns go by faster. I strongly dislike the three minute turns in dominion where one player is playing tons of random crap, drawing, and in the end buys a colony and then the next person does the same thing. This game gets rid of that. Games last roughly twenty minutes and all of it is interesting.

Without the two buy rule there is significantly less decision, but with two buys it is often unclear what the optimal play is. I find that this rule makes the game remain short yet have as much strategy and meaningful decisions per minute as game I have played.

RATING: 8
2/17/2014: I lost to my 13-year-old...again. She is still undefeated. What's her strategy? She mimics me. Instead of reading the cards, she assumes that anything I buy will be a good choice. So she buys them too. Then she gets a better roll than me and it's all over too quickly. It's time to make her play with the advanced rules. With the simple rules, by the time you feel like you've built a fairly good deck of dice, it's already over. I still love this game and I am always wanting to play it. It's basically crack for a lover of dice games.

I am enough of a fan of this game that I even picked up two of the expansions after only playing it once.  It's a deck building game, but with dice.  The core game doesn't have a ton of depth to it and there is a lot of luck involved.  But that essentially makes it a good casual game where it could be anybody's victory at any time.  I like to keep a good selection of games where the experienced players and the new comers are just about on even ground.  This is an excellent example of that type of game.  In the midst of all the luck of the draw and the roll, there still is enough decision making to make it strategic as well.  Like with any good deck building style game, if you don't stack your deck with the good stuff and clean out the garbage, you won't have much of a chance.  I love this game.  I love the dice.  I love the character cards.  And I love that it pays fairly quickly (under an hour).

RATING: 7
Very unique, first of its genre and probably not the last or the best, but a pioneer with a great deal of strategy coupled with the gambling instinct of rolling tons of beautiful custom dice.  Built-in player interaction in monsters attacking each-other for victory points and "culling" (trashing) die from your bag are both interesting strategies not present in mainstream deck-building games, which this strongly resembles (notably Dominion).  Dominion is the stronger game but I still heartily recommend Quarriors when your deck-builder loving group wants something a little different.

EDIT: So apparently in "May 2012" I just decided to transcribe Tom Vasel's opinions as my own on these reviews.  Bumping this one down a point, it isn't that great.

RATING: 6.8
The game delivers exactly what it promises. Dicebuilding! It has the same selling points a game like Dominion has but in additions it has tons of dice. And dice are cool. I like the design where the dice are used for different varieties of monsters/spells. This keeps the components managable while preserving the most possible variety. As you can expect with a dice game there is some luck involved but mostly this will even out in the end. Mostly the player who recognizes the best possible combos in the dice pool will win against somebody just rolling well. The only complaints I have is that the dice are too small and the game is over too quickly. But the last issue can be solved by simply playing to a higher amount of points. The first one is harder to solve.

Game did not hit the table anymore. Had to go.

RATING: 6
Dominion is still my only real experience with deck-building, so I'm not burned out. In fact I'm still in love with the mechanism. However I've been reluctant to venture into any of the other games with this mechanism because I usually hear that Dominion just does it better. However, when I heard "dice-building game," that seemed different enough to hook me. I've read a lot of opinions on this game, and most of them seem to be in the love-it or hate-it camp, but I guess I'm one of the rare cases because I'm just kind of indifferent towards the game. I want to love it because the mechanism is so enjoyable and I love dice-rolling games. In fact this game has provided plenty of smiles already as I see people get that great roll (or that horrendous roll.) Yet I find myself often frustrated with the execution. The smaller dice are annoying, and the bags are horrible, but my #1 complaint is simply the cards. I mean, I like the fact that they provide variety, but since key information that players need on almost every turn are on those cards it can be a real pain having them in the middle of the table being shared by all the players. Also, since there are multiple versions of each creature/spell, you can't easily memorize what one does because it may be different from the way it was the last game. My final complaint is that the endgame is somewhat anti-climactic. By the very nature of the point-scoring a player will just be sitting there for an entire round with enough points to win and it becomes a round of "is someone lucky enough to stop her?" Some of these issues may become less noticeable and frustrating with further plays, but for now I just can't get on the love-it train.

RATING: 6
Score: 6, based on original rules.  Score: 6, based on variant rules.  Maybe culling needs to be easier than assistants only?

Note to self: try variant where creatures return to the Wilds when scored (you do not have to cull them.)  Scoring still allows culling.  You may buy any number of creature dice per turn but only 1 spell die per turn (spells get buffed since they do not return to the Wilds.)  Allow re-roll of dice by expending 1 die.  Flip 9 creatures and 4 spells during set-up.  Depletion of 4 creature piles ends the game.  Start player gets -1 Quid, 2nd 0, 3rd +1, 4th +2.

Suffers a bit from down-time, since people have to draw and roll but maybe it was the group I was playing with.  The rules should have been designed so that you don't need to worry about your "discard" pile, so you can always be immediately ready to draw.

The summon creatures then buy doesn't work that well, since people need to know what they're going to do with the quiddity before they summon.

There is a lack of combos, since at least Nightfall you need to find what colors you want to combo.  (And Martial Law gives you a bunch of Kickers.)

4 plays.  First game, I was player 1 and I won.  Second game, player 2 won.  (I was player 4.)

3rd play: variant, 3rd player won.

4th play: original, 2nd player (me) won.

For the 4th play, people agreed the game might benefit from the designer's culling variant.

Game suffers from a bit of downtime.

Rolling poorly sucks, of course.  Multiple buys definitely makes the game better, IMO.  Definitely seems to be a runaway leader problem.

Declaring an attack value is easy and convenient.

I do like the idea of multiple buys, discarding a die to re-roll any number of dice (or just one), and Assistants being the only dice that cull when scoring.  Limiting creatures to killing only one creature may slow down the game, because then each die would have to declare what its attacking.  Perhaps big creatures should be limited to killing one creature, and you just "chump block" with assistants or some such.

Multiple buys may nerf big creatures, but is the cumulative +1 cost per additional die necessary?

RATING: 8
Quarriors is a dice lover’s paradise: you collect buckets of dice and roll them, the more the better. Therefore, as usual when dice are involved, there’s no foolproof strategy a bad roll can’t spoil but who cares? The real fun here is rolling dice and see what happens.

Comparisions with Dominion are incorrect and correct at the same time: actually both games make for a deck/pool building customization (and here surely Quarriors owes much to Dominion), yet where Dominion enables to reduce randomness to an acceptable level anyhow, Quarriors’ strength is right in the unpredictability of every roll.

Undoubtedly Quarriors is far from perfect but as undoubtedly it makes for a great filler as well.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
So yeah, Quarriors. I'm a Eurogamer and I'm liable to look at any blatant Dominion clone as just that. Whether Thunderstone ("It's Dominion as a dungeon delve!"), Puzzle Strike ("It's Dominion with chips! And you can hurt people!") or Quarriors ("It's Dominion with dice! And you can hurt people!"), these are all clearly derivative, and not nearly as good, as the original. Not to say I dislike them, but I value simplicity and originality, and they simply don't measure up to Dominion (and I'm not even a huge Dominion fan).

Well, one can try to rate games based on how objectively "good" a game is, judging only the qualities one sees in a rulebook, or one can rate games based on the experiences playing it. I know people such as the former, who will play a game with me and laugh and have a rollicking good time with it, then go on BGG the next day and rate it 4/10. I don't quite get that, you had a great time and the game directly facilitated that. If I played a game and had fun, I'll give the game credit for that.

Based on the former "objective" rating (well, based on my subjective attempts to be objective), I'd probably rate Quarriors 6/10 on BGG. But it [i]is[/i] fun. The dice create tension -- can I roll well enough to destroy his creatures and keep him from winning? -- and moments of groans, though unfortunately not necessarily matching cheers.

Like Dominion, the variability helps the game shine. It is clever how the same dice can represent different creatures game to game, to add more variability to a game that only has a very limited number of differing dice. But even with many plays it is impossible to remember what, for example, the Questing Wizard does, because there's three different versions of the Wizard with one to three different special powers each. Which one is in this game? Read the wall of text on the card in the middle of the table, with no helpful icons or even bolded keywords like "When Scoring", "When Summoned" or "When Destroyed" to remind you when you need to care about those. This is a single point of frustration with the game, but it's a big one, and one that will likely keep me playing the iOS version more than face to face.

Well, the game IS fun. Maybe more outright, obvious fun than Dominion. I borrowed it to show my 8yo son, who quickly gets bored of any simple German-style game I put in front of him, and he quickly took to this and it is now probably his favourite game. The game works very well as a 2-player game, though 3p is likely ideal.

The game comes with two advanced variants. Normally this sort of thing I wouldn't even look twice at, but you should look at these. One grants an extra buy on your turn, which is such an obvious improvement without increasing complexity that it should be the standard way of playing for everyone. The other transforms the game from a quick "what the hell" game to a more drawn-out "work your way up" game; both work well and one should feel comfortable playing whichever sounds more suitable to their play styles.

The game is not polished. It has many rough edges, but it is from these rough edges that much of the fun emerges. When I try to look at this objectively, I must concede that Dominion is the better game, and the one I would probably be first to choose among it and its many descendants. But with my son I have played this many times and have not tired of it, so its rating shoots up in my book.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
Updated to a 10 because it is better than everything.  Tons of variability in game play, no two games are alike.

Quarriors is a deckbuilding game but with dice.  You start with a set of 12 basic terrible die and work to remove "bad" die in order to purchase a better overall set.  Dragons, wizards, and other awesome creature die do various attack and damage values and score you glory (points) as you go.  Throw some spells into your set in order to add other perks and upgrades.  

Very cool concept that produces balanced play, multiple paths to victory, and limitless replayability/variation.  The expansions open up the game even more and I purchased Quarmageddon after only 5 plays of the original.  It's that good.  I had to have more.  

Note: Quarmageddon comes with a very valuable tray to hold all of the dice.  Without the expansion they are stored in tiny baggies that will have you sneakily leaving the room when finished so your buddy has to clean up the game.

Con's - I docked one point for a few reasons.  Some of the die can be overpowering.  The scorecard is bad.  The container to store everything in the original game is pathetic.  Some of the effects of the creature cards can be confusing and/or overwhelming to where it slows gameplay.  Constantly refreshing what the various die capabilities are.

All in all -  AWESOME GAME which led to insta-addiction for me.

RATING: 7
B&N.  Finally got it to the table after many months on the shelf.  Enjoyable game.  Very simple, and sort of a preamble to the dice masters.  The long box storage is very nice.  I am currently using the quartifacts box to store my D and D dice masters.

If you are choosing between this or dice masters, it's a tough call.  Quarriors is self contained and easy to teach.  Non-collectible, so no worries about hunting rare cards.  Quarriors come with decent draw bags, which dice masters does not.  My small hands can barely reach into those dice masters bags.  What Dice Masters gives that Quarriors does not, is deeper strategy.  Up front customization of your team and more complicated battle makes dice masters a lot more strategic and deep (relatively).

RATING: 9
Have only played twice now but first thoughts are there is more strategy than it looks at first and that it's very equivalent in all respect to Dominion (just as light with just as many decisions and just as much player interaction). The dice are very cool and there's lots of variability in the set up each game. Promos are cool and looking forward to more expansions (which I hear are in the works already). The game plays fairly quickly even with 4 players at around 30 minutes, even less at times. Pretty fun little game and if you like deck building games it can hold it's own with any of them.

Edit after 2nd day of gaming and 6 more games under my belt. This game is absolutely wonderful! There is waaaay more depth and strategy than the game shows at first. My group has been having an absolute blast with this game. For example on the last roll or the last game tonight everyone was standing up, leaning over the table as I rolled my dice. When I pulled out the 3 Glory points for the win they all jumped up and laughed. Lots of pats on the back and "great job!" all around. Fun, fun, game. Very tense and just...well, fun. Don't see this one going away anytime soon. We're all hooked on it and we're finding lots of different strategies to win all the time.

RATING: 5
Well I prefer it to Dominion (although that's not saying much as I really don't like Dominion). 

It's too light and the dice icons are too tiny for my eyes, is that a '1' or a 2 etc (making the pictures bigger on the cards at least would have helped!)

I like the idea - and a bag of dice is so much more tactile and fun than 'shuffle' 'shuffle' shuffle'

Luck of the roll is obviously present - but that isn't my problem with the game, the fact you're limited to l die purchase a turn and have nothing else to do with the resource means there is little reason not to buy the most expensive die you can each turn.

Add the 'rich get richer' element of culling your used dice and the whole experience is a bit meh ... everything is rather obvious.

So in summary - nothing objectionable, but nothing grabs me to want to play again (or own a copy). Solid '5'

RATING: 7.5
This was a delightful surprise. I wasn't a big fan of Dominion, and after being on the fence for a few months I had finally decided I wasn't going to get this, but my husband surprised me with it (plus all expansions!) for my birthday. It's really a fun, light game with a much more attractive theme, much more interactive gameplay, and a much less fiddly mechanic than Dominion (no shuffling!).

I really like that you score your creatures and then remove them at the beginning of your turn: it makes you less attached to your dice so you (almost) never get upset when they get killed off during your opponents' attack. The spells and special creature abilities are very cool, and the dice look awesome, even if a little on the cheap side.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
I do like Quarriors quite a bit, but there are degenerate creature layouts. The Dragon can be a problem generally, especially the one that can't be attacked by anything less than level 3 given that means you are only vulnerable to other dragons. It's possible to get lucky and get one on your first turn, which can be nasty. Also, the Ghost that allows you to burn basic Quiddity dice to keep alive can be extremely powerful. I'm probably 10 games in and have seen a couple unsatisfying mixes, so it's not a huge problem - but it does come up. Like Thunderstone, you're going to want to keep an eye on the mix of creatures & spells once you get into the game and make sure they look reasonable. A few creatures may end up getting banned around here. In general, I'm not sure I'm totally comfortable with some variants of the Questing Wizard and Quake Dragon.

Also, this game is very fast. You're probably only buying 6-10 dice before you need to have everything important to your mix. Long-term basically does not exist. Which is fine, this is a game that needs to be short, but you need to be aware of it.

We played a couple 4-player games where we played to 15 instead of 12, and that seemed to work pretty well. The game seemed to play out more satisfactorily.
---
3 players seems to be the sweet spot for me out of the box; the 4-player game seems to be over to soon. Last time we played with four there was the suggestion that we should simply play to 15 points (the 3-player target) instead of 12 with 4 players, and to me that sounds right. We'll give that a try next time.
---
Quarriors has a lot going for it: fast, fun, lots of dice. As I play it more, I see a few patterns that some gamers are obviously not going to like: rolling well (getting a lot of Quiddity during your first time through your pool, mainly) can be a big deal. Getting early scores, when culling can help you a lot, can be similarly swingy. You can buy big creatures and then due to the luck of the dice they never show.

You know what, though? Dominion and Thunderstone have the exact same issues. Quarriors just makes them appear more significant because of the dice luck. You're going to see powerful cards in Dominion or Thunderstone. Often as not you may not be able to use them situationally, but at least you saw them.

Anyway, Quarriors is a dice game, and it has properties of dice games. You want to put yourself in the best position to succeed, knowing that the dice may not come through. You want to identify good risks. Like Dominion and Thunderstone, you need to identify powerful but simple and workable combinations, manage your ratio of creatures to Quiddity. Unlike Dominion and Thunderstone, Quarriors really does play in 20 minutes and gives you a lot of bang for your gaming buck in terms of interest, excitement, and engagement.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 6
Pretty darn cool idea!  You can't deny the absolute awesomeness of the product!  The dice are just plain sweet!

However, I didn't enjoy the gameplay quite as much as many deck-building games it was based on; I felt like there weren't as many opportunities for dice synergies as there are for synergistic decks in many deck-building games.  There also seem to be turn order issues and potential runaway leader problems.  Perhaps this will be improved through expansions.

Overall, I'd probably give the gameplay around a 5 or 6, but I'll stick with 6 due to the sheer impressiveness of the product!  One of those weird games where the components really make the game, despite the shortcomings of the game itself.

RATING: 3
[i]1 play.

This was a another new-to-me game that is not strictly.  It is a classic snowball, rich get-richer game.  With dice!  Those who know me well know that dice games are not my strong suit, and though we played a longer session (playing to 15 points, I believe) I was still completely shut out at game end, due to some unfortunate die rolling.

[thing=91536][/thing] is not exactly a bad game; lots of modern Euro games are mindless snowballing points engines. And for those who enjoy the cartoonish art work and the lampooning of classic fantasy genres, it could be a pleasant and enjoyable experience. However, this is not a game for me. I enjoy quite a bit more thinking in my games. Although the [thing=36218]Dominion[/thing]-esque nature of the game was expected, the volatility of the game that accompanied the die rolling was not. I had hoped it would provide a little more player control to enable true competition.  I hesitate to even call the decision-space tactical, as the options seemed too few and too arbitrary from turn to turn.  Although the game was not unpleasant in any way, this is not a game I will need to try again.  [b][BGCOLOR=#ff66cc][COLOR=#ff66cc]_[/COLOR]4[COLOR=#ff66cc]_[/COLOR][/BGCOLOR][/b].

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 4
This isn't a bad choice if you want some mindless fun. But it is just that - mindless.

The creature system is an interesting way to try and provide conflict, but still doesn't end up feeling THAT interactive since once you get a dude out there's usually nothing you can do for him except hope. Maybe you can cast a spell.

This game compounds luck with yet more luck. Not only can you draw poorly, but you can roll poorly too. This sounds like the main problem with the game but unfortunately there are far more insidious problems at work here...

First, there really isn't much in the way of interesting decisions. The first game might seem fun but I assure you with each successive play and the more you explore the deck your interest will dwindle. The only real choice to make on any given turn is what to buy, and choices tend to be pretty obvious. Unlike most deck builders, this is not a combo building game. Cards effects just don't connect in interesting ways.

Second, the game has a runaway leader problem. First person to have a couple good creatures survive (getting good creatures and getting them out are both the result of luck) gets some VP and gets to cull some dice, making them more likely to get more creatures out to get more VP and make it harder for anyone else to get a creature out so that they can get some VP and cull some dice. And if you're playing in a game with a Dragon? Might as well call it game when someone buys one, they're just going to win anyway.

We tried playing a couple games to double VP to help with the runaway leader issue. It helped a little, but not enough.

Third, almost everything about the balance just feels off. The creatures that score their owners the most VP are also the hardest for anyone else to kill. I get the thinking behind it, but in practice it just doesn't work. Especially because how did they get that creature again? Oh right they got a lucky roll.

On the plus side, all the colored dice are very pretty.

RATING: 6
Meet Quarriors: Dominion’s dice cousin! The way to build your deck reminds me quite a lot of Dominion. However, it’s not quite the same, as you create monsters which can attack and will score VPs if they are alive when it’s your turn again. That’s a completely different mechanic than that of Dominion, and I like this aggressiveness.

I honestly didn’t think that I would like it as much as I did as I’m not a big fan of dice based games, but this was a pleasant surprise when you take it for what it is; you cannot win with a great strategy if you roll badly and vice versa, but it’s okay as it’s a very quick game. I would buy it if I could solve these two things: it would probably not be played as much because of the theme and the fact that Dominion is easier to introduce to nongamers, and then because of the awful box which doesn’t fit on my shelves at all...

RATING: 4
Quarriors (no explanation point from me...maybe for Age of Steam(!) but never for a game like this) is a dice based resource management game that has some similarities to the deck building games that have been common recently.

Painting with broad strokes, players start out with dice that produce the basic resource for the game. Those dice are then used to buy more dice, which can have the ability to produce more resources, to give the players special powers, or which can be turned into beasties which score VPs if they live an entire round.

Beasties have a basic power and toughness stat, and any of them that are summoned during a player's turn immediately go on the rampage. Their power stat is summed, and then goes up against each other player's creatures clockwise around the table, with each player putting forward defenders one at a time (cue mental image of an army at the gates demanding a champion and some poor sap being hurriedly shoved out the door to get pounded with its companions hoping that the attackers get tired from beating on the poor sod so hard), if their defence is equal to or lower than the remaining power of the attacker, they die, go into the used pool and temporarily reduce the offensive power of the attacker, though attackers never suffer damage or death. Basically, the entire goal is to get creatures to come out, make the rounds killing off the potential point scoring pieces from the other players, and to then survive long enough to score for you. Most everything else is just fluff or extra special rules.

The end of all of this seems like it can anti-climatically come rather soon, and a good deal of the ability to score points seems to hinge on getting the right mix of monsters to resources into the bag, then pulling out the right mix of dice, and then getting the right stuff to roll so you can put out a decent-sized army, trash everyone elses' stuff, and then score a lot of points in the process. Probabilities can be used to make this more likely, but there are still a lot of random factors that go into it. Not really offensive, and easy to play after the initial game, but nothing that speaks to me in any particular way either. Not dissimilar to Roll Through the Ages for me, a not particularly good game that is saved from being outright terrible mostly by being short.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Quarriors has left me with a very unique impression.  At first glance, I thought the game would be pretty bad.  But after playing it, I was pleasantly surprised and found the mechanics to be quite engaging.  The game feels much more interactive than your standard deck-builder because you're rolling and adding dice to your bag, and the dice add randomness.

The big problem I have with the game is that due to the low amount of Victory Points needed to win, it boils down to who gets the first big set of creatures to stick.  In that regard, the game is almost strictly luck-based, and it puts a damper on what could be a more strategic element.  The 15 point scoreboard is just too low.  Increasing it to 20 helps, but even then sometimes the person who would've gotten the 15 points, also qualifies for 20 since he'll get 8 victory points for his big group of creatures.  When someone reaches 15 or 20, the game feels like it's over prematurely and most of the time you don't get to use your cool combos yet.

I'd like to try this one at say, 40 victory points.  That would give all of the players time to catch up, and maybe see a few lead swings.  It would also allow a player's special combos to shine, such as the ones that destroy opponent creatures when entering play.

I like the game a lot, but the victory point total needs to be modified for me to justify playing it.

RATING: 6
Not sure if I'd want to play face to face, but the iOS app is kinda fun. Although I can't figure out what it doesn't auto defend when there are no choices to be me made--which is most of the time. It's basically a deck building, but with dice instead of cards, and the dice get rolled giving you potentially different "versions" of the same card. But the new thing here is that most of the cards you buy are "creatures", which you can put into play, and score VP for you if they're still alive when your next turn comes around. And every time you put creatures into play, they attack all other creatures in play (creatures having both attack/defense values), so you don't really want to put creatures into play unless you can kill other creatures in play, or more commonly, expect them to survive coming back around to you.

RATING: 7
SECOND EDITION RULES COMMENTS:

This is a great game for young teens.  It crosses both the fun of Dominion strategy with light combat with special powers.  There is a lot of luck in what dice come out when and what faces are available to be used, so it isn't real deep in strategy.  However, the game play is simple enough to get younger people involved.  

I've updated my rating to a 7 from a 4.

ORIGINAL FIRST EDITION RULES COMMENTS:

Comments based on 2 plays with 1st Edition rules.

This game is really not for me.  A bit too heavy on the luck spectrum and not enough options per turn.  Nice concepts, but the flow/purpose/options needs some work.

I'll stick with Dominion

RATING: 8.25
I’ll admit it, I like rolling some dice.  I enjoy it in my RPGs and I enjoy it in a lot of my games.  I also highly enjoy card games.  They’ve mixed both fairly well into Quarriors!  It’s not a perfect game; you’ll bump into mechanics that’ll make you stumble.  Other players will help you move past them though, and sitting down to enjoy a light game, such as this, is never bad.  

It has a fast pace, more-so once you’ve learned the cards, and gives the feel of just a light-hearted time.  The games can take as fast as 10 minutes with the right dice on the field, and if you can get the players into the feel of the game they’ll be cheers and laughter.  I have a blast playing this game anytime of the day.

RATING: 4
There's a visceral enjoyment in playing with colored dice, and the game itself is rather more fun than I would have guessed going into it: I'm not generally a fan of "see what happens" games, and there's an awful lot of "see what happens" here. (The two major decisions in the game are "which rolled monsters do I pay to summon?" and "what do I buy?", which seem to often have straightforward answers.) And the "no shuffling" isn't a selling point for me; I actually find the physical mechanisms of Dominion faster than those of Quarriors. 

But nonetheless: oddly fun, despite all the randomness.

[8/11: Another 3 plays and my interest in the game as published is waning fast. Adding a few house rules has helped keep it engaging, though.]

RATING: 8
This is a gorgeous game with a TON of dice to roll, if that's your thing then you'll love this.

The quality of all the components is fantastic, I really like the metal tin that it all comes in (shaped and designed to look like one of the dice from the game).

It has some minor flaws, there's not that much room for a grand strategy in the base game, generally buying the most expensive choices you can afford each round (with some exceptions) will help you to victory, and by its nature there is a lot of randomness. If you don't mind that and want something very tactile that's fast and fun I highly recommend.

Update: After a third play, my conclusion is the game is decent, but not something that I will ever desire to own or request again.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6.5
(Febrer 2016)
Entenc tots els comentaris i crítiques del joc. I entenc la proposta dels propis dissenyadors de que cadascú alteri el joc amb les seves home-rules.
Quarriors és una idea boníssima que van perfilar i perfeccionar molt amb el Dice Masters, però que al Quarriors li falten coses per polir. 
Com a resum: les partides són massa llargues pel nivell de frustració que "pots" acumular. A banda, algunes cartes tenen molt de text, que pots passar per alt...
 
És aviat per pronunciar-me, però potser faltaria algun mecanisme per forçar el canvi d'una cara. Això és degut a que pots comprar tropocentes criatures i que quan les tires no et surtin les seves cares. Si no et surt la cara de criatura, no pots puntuar ni "repartir" --> Frustració.

La advanced culling rule evita l'efecte bola de neu (runnaway leader) i s'agraeix la seva presència. Potser caldria incloure, a més, algun altre mecanisme de catch-up. P.ex. explicat aquí: https://boardgamegeek.com/article/21964525#21964525

Una "variant" sobre la advanced culling rule és que a la fase 1 (score), pots decidir NO puntuar un dau. Si no el puntues, va a Used i pots destruir (cull) un altre dau de la Used pile.

La advanced buying rule (2 compres enlloc d'1) és una molt bona implementació ja que ofereix una alternativa en l'estratègia de compra (un bitxo gros? o dos més petits?), però per contra fa que algun bitxo que donava 1 compra extra ara no sigui tant interessant. Es podria substituir aquesta compra extra d'aquests bitxos per +1 Buy/+1 Quiddity.

Se m'ocurreixen mil idees. Però no cal oblidar que és i ha de seguir sent un filler ràpid. 
1) Al DM quan tires els daus tens 1 re-roll de tots els daus que vulguis. Es podria aplicar, però ja ralentitzarà més. Hi ha qui aplica: "gasta 1 dau qualsevol (spent) per fer un re-roll dels daus que vulguis" (podent-se fer tants cops com vulguis mentre et quedin daus).
2) Lo important és poder treure algun bitxo en un determinat moment (per puntuar i per repartir estopa als altres). Per tant, lo de "provocar" un canvi de cara d'un dau de criatura és algo a estudiar (veure historial partides on hi ha comentaris):
2.1) Hi ha la variant Quarks: gasta 6 Quarks per forçar una cara (d'un dau en active pool). Quan has comprat 1 criatura, els Quiddity sobrants els converteixes en Quarks. Es necessiten fitxes (o un D20 per jugador). Per canviar la cara, cal haver anat "guardant" quarks.
2.2) I una meva: gasta 3 daus qualsevol (spent) per forçar la cara d'un dau en l'active pool. --> Es pot forçar en qualsevol torn.
PROBLEMA: pot haver-hi daus xungos, que al sortir ho petin. P.ex., hi ha un Spell que dóna 3 de Gloria. Si et dediques a "forçar-lo" a cada torn que et surt, pot destrossar la partida, amb lo que la meva alternativa (2.2) potser no és massa adequada.

Last time played with 3 this time with 4 and I think there is a scaling problem.  With more players, you suffer more attacks and late game only the highest defense monsters survive.  Thus the game locked up until someone got lucky enough to roll their high defense monsters.  Felt wrong.

RATING: 4
Quarriors takes the super-trendy deck-building mechanic and replaces the cards with dice. While in theory this could be an interesting sort of game, and I can appreciate the design on a general level, it is essentially just a reimplementation of the general “Lets Make a Game! About Dice!” idea that generally fails to work for me. 

Really though, this is probably just another game that was not designed for me. I am not a fan of fillers in general, and dice games in particular. The main reason I ended up trying it out was it was new, short, and hot enough that it seemed like it was worth trying a few times. It ultimately was, but I saw enough in those three games that I know this game is not for me. 
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
Players are Quarriors, warriors fighting for something beginning with "Q" ... couldn't get into the back story!  A fairly simple deck building game where players draw six dice from their bag and roll them.  The results are applied - you may roll Quiddity (money), which can be spent on a new die, and/or on generating creatures that sit in front of you and try to beat up your opponents creatures.  Any creature still in front of you on a future turn allows you to gain glory and cull useless dice from your collection.  First to a set Glory threshold wins.  Nothing outstanding here, certainly light, a lot of luck, but it's fast and fun in a way with the quirky die rolling mechanic.

RATING: 6
Interesting take on deck building games, but with some twists that are either cool or annoying. Games can be quite the blowout (my first game ended with one player with 15 points while i wasn't even on the board) mostly because of the double random nature of the game: you draw your dices from a bag and then roll them, which can mean that not only you might not get the needed die when you need it, but you might not even be able to roll a creature, which are needed to score points. You also need to roll the correct amount of points to put the creature into play. Mindless fun, but with loads of luck.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 3
Roll dice, beat up everyone's creatures, buy a die. Wait. Wait. Wait. Roll dice, beat up everyone's creatures, buy a die. Wait. Wait. Wait.

Quarriors (what a name...) is a pale pale shadow of Dominion. It features dice and fighting so it's somehow "better, funner, cooler" for the typical gamer.
 
There's a lot of important things missing here. Real decisions are really scarce. You spend the game reacting to dice and rarely taking any initiative. A great example of "The Game Plays You" in action.

Combos occur very infrequently, since the die must come up at the right time and also then roll it on the right face. Unlike a card, you can't count a result by "waiting for the card" to come up. You can "stack the deck" all you want and buy lots of a given die, but who is to say it will roll as you wish?

Also I don't see the satisfaction in victory. So... I rolled great a few times! Where's the consequence? 

Not only is a session not memorable, it's not engrossing in tactical thinking like Dominion or Lost Cities. Even a light auction game like Ra or For Sale has delicious tension. Very blah. 

Overall a fad that should pass by autumn.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

My biggest beef with the game is also an asset in a way. The game plays itself to some degree. There isn't much in the way of choices to make...You buy the most expensive thing out there; You have to attack every turn; You have to score your creatures at the start of your turn. With each new expansion and with optional variant rules that does change a bit, but more rules makes it harder to teach and more choices makes the game drag and it is one that shouldn't drag.

Quarriors had a strange arc with me. When I first picked it up, I was largely dissatisfied with how the game played out - the dice mechanic seemed interesting, but rounds were dull and repetitive.

Then Dice Masters came out and I was like "Oh, so this is how the game is supposed to be designed." I picked up a few sets, which I thoroughly enjoyed.

Maybe after having enjoyed Dice Masters, I could find some joy in Quarriors. I'll never know now, as I simply cannot find the wherewithal to break it out again.

RATING: 8.5
Quarriors is an excellent deck-building type dice game, where your "deck" consists of a dice pool that you draw from a bag.  Players roll their basic starting dice to gain "quiddity", the game's main commodity, and use that to purchase creature or spell dice to add to their pool.  Creatures are then used to attack the creatures of other players with spells providing augmenting effects.  Your creatures that survive to the next round are then scored.  

Quarriors is a fairly light game, and, of course being a dice game, comes with a high amount of luck.  Not only is there the luck of the draw from your dice pool, but also the luck of the roll to see what results you get.  That being said, I find Quarriors very fun for what it is.  It is also highly replayable as there are many versions of creatures and spells that offer different special abilities.  The game is also customizeable with many variants out there to make it a shorter/longer game with different options for culling.  There are several expansions that add even more creatures and spells to create more replayability.  Our group loves this game, and I highly recommend it.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 3
Alright, this game has been on my mind most of the summer.  My husband heard about it on BGG and placed an order through our FLGS.  We were the only ones to order it and did manage to get our copy before they became next to impossible to get up here in Canada.  Over the past two weeks, I've played multiple matches with every possible group dynamic (2p,3p,4p).  Here's what I've discovered:

This game looks super cool for the most part and everyone who sees it is eager to try it.  The monster and spell cards are really neat and the corresponding dice are really snazzy.  I like that there are monster and spell 'classes', so you have tons of replay, as the dice can match up to a varied number of cards.  

However, the path to glory track (or the scorecard) is absolute junk.  The dice bags and player pieces are also incredibly low quality and some of the colors are difficult to distinguish.  So if you're playing 4p, it's next to impossible to tell which cube is blue and which cube is black.  

Now for the gameplay:  I found the game to be absolutely dull 2p, kind of blah on 3p, and the most fun, but still not great with 4p.  The issue isn't the game is too short (which I've read many people mention).  The problem instead is what it sells itself as: a dice building game.  This is no such thing.  None of the dice 'work' together or create any sort of satisfying combo like a deck in Dominion or Thunderstone.  You are simply buying dice that you hope will posssibly be drawn together but you have no guarantee that when you do play the dice you will get the outcome you desire.  

So we've found, it's easiest to win the game by focusing on whatever dice is available that scores the most glory and costs the least amount of quiddity to purchace.  Ignore any spells, purchase 2 or so of those dice, hope they roll on the proper side and you win every time.  People that attempt to play the game like a 'deck building' game and get an engine going just get super frustrated that the game is over before they get a chance to roll their defense spell dice with their super powerful attacker.

This game is just a gimmick and once the novelty wears off, there is nothing left.  Save yourself the money and pass.

RATING: 8
If this was made by Rio Grande, this would have been called Domion Dice. Do I really need to say more about the gameplay? In Dominion there's really two luck points: drawing your good cards, and drawing the synergies you were hoping to build. On top of that, you have now added the luck of the draw. I thought the game may have eneded a little too fast. The fact that the best creatures score the most points is a little confusing to me. Why not have the wimps score a lot of points and the brutes score just a few points? Part of me thought the game should have been a little longer, but the other half says "dice games should be short." It seems that the first person to score a monster is going to win the game. At least with the 12 points required in a four player game. 

Ultimately your enjoyment and preference of this game is going to be based on your outlook. If you're looking for the strategy of Dominion, you are most likely going to be underwhelmed. If you are looking for a quirky dice game, you are in the right place. 

7->8 I've made peace with the chaotic luck. You have to accept the best strategy is not a promise to win, you have to have a good (or the best) strategy and hope the dice go your way.

RATING: 6.5
I don't know how I feel about Quarriors.  I love the idea of a dice building game, but I don't really like the combat. I love the components (SO MANY DICE) but the fact that they are dice brings a lot of limiting factors into the game.  For one, you have to constantly reference the card to see which version you are playing with. For two, the cost of custom dice molds really limit the amount of options you are given and the amount that can be introduced in later expansions. Also, you have to have a high tolerance for randomness to really enjoy the game.  Not a bad game, but not in my top list.

RATING: 6

I strongly encourage you to play to a higher VP count.  It's a fun filler, and a good game for introducing new gamers to (as they've got as good a chance at winning as everyone else), but there's not a lot of meat to it.

RATING: 6.5
Rating based on 2 plays:

It's Dominion with dice instead of cards. Like most dice games, it's completely luck based, except for when you choose which dice to add to your bag which is only mostly luck based.

You know what though? I still find the game fun. It has a good amount of that built-in tension that comes with most dice games. The gorgeous components don't hurt much either.

What the game can really use is another way to cull dice, perhaps something similar to resting from Thunderstone.

Looking forward to any expansions, particularly those that would add new classes of dice in the mix.

UPDATE: After three plays, the most enjoyable game I had was without Dragons. Still needs improvement on the culling mechanism however. On further thought, you should be allowed to cull a die for its cost plus a premium.

UPDATE 3/4/14: Downgraded to 6.5. I still enjoy the game. And dice. So many custom dice. Allowing to purchase multiple dice on a turn improves things some as well. Haven't played tournament style yet (return scoring dice to the wilds). I'd love to see how that changes things.

RATING: 6.6
[b]Traded[/b]
I did like Quarriors as a decent idea, but never really played it, so it was one of the earliest games to leave my collection.
[b]previous comment:[/b]

The popular opinion on Board Game Geek is that Marvel Dice Masters is a better game than Quarriors.  I disagree.  Quarriors has an elegant system, with reasonably straightforward interactions between the cards.  It's a light, quick, silly fun game.

Marvel Dice Masters, on the other hand, has tons of ambiguities in the timing of the abilities, and is completely dominated gameplay-wise by a handful of cheap cards.  The meta-game is kinda lousy, because the game is so imbalanced.

As an idea of how much better I think Quarriors is, the only way I've found to enjoy MDM is to modify the rules to be more Quarriors-like - using a common pool from which both players purchase dice.

RATING: 6
Played 6 times with 2&3 players for 3.25 hours.

I liked this more than I thought I would based on complaints I read on-line. Yes there is a lot of luck, but I felt like is was *not* obvious what to do every turn. There was scope for decisions on buying dice, culling dice, and even how to use the powers of the dice and which to keep to check the leader - again, decent amount of decisions for a light filler game. And the game looks fantastic.

So far 3 of my buddies are either lukewarm or cold to this game. This does not bode well. They didn't even get the initial novelty enjoyment that I did. I think the idea of the game is good, but the execution needs a bit of tweaking. After plays #5 and #6, I have to drop my rating to a 7. I want to like this game.

RATING: 5
On the date, 7/20/11, I own and/or have played all of the deck building games available with the exception of one.  

The others have cards or pogs, this has dice.  I generally don't like dice games.  This is a little different because you are buying a specific die which you roll for the options on that die.  For instance, one of the various creature dice might have "buys" or "attacks" (both with varying strengths), and the roll of the die determines which action you get.

In all deck building games, an important strategy is to get rid of as much of your starting hand as possible because these are low-end starting cards that eventually bog down the hand.  A nice feature of this game is that when you score, you have the option of discarding dice, which often occurs as early as the second turn.

It is a quick game, 10-30 mins, so a good filler.  It comes in a compact metal box and will hold some sure-to-come expansions.

The important numbers on the dice are small and hard to read for those with poor sight.  Since I generally don't like dice games, and this is no exception, it doesn't score high with me.  Other players were also lukewarm, but one did like it.

Edit:  I have played 6 games, and in every game the person who went first, won.

RATING: 5
Only a few games in, but had a lot of fun. Dice hate me, so this purchase raised eyebrows in my group, but it's more about the journey and the laughs than outright wins. I'd totally recommend this to fans of dice games, but if you don't like luck, steer clear (though I would assume that should be clear). There is the odd strategic decision to make, but a lot of your fate is trusted to the dice.

We suspect a problem with the culling mechanic that might need to be house-ruled, but for the most part it's a silly, fun dicefest. Impossible to like if you try to take it seriously (strategy takes a backseat to luck), but if you just kick back and let it happen, it can be quite fun.

Downgraded from 8 to 7 after a few more plays.

EDIT - I might have dropped this further, but the "advanced rules" really saved this one. Use them: when you would score, you can choose to cull or not. If you cull, you get the glory, if you don't, it just goes to your used pile and you get no glory. You now have 2 captures per turn instead of 1. Makes the game longer, but a lot better IMO.

RATING: 8
This is my first true dice-centered game and so far I am enjoying the experience, though I have very few plays under my belt. The rating may go up or down in the next few months as I try it out with different groups of friends, but at this point I would not turn down a chance to give it a few rounds. 

My wife and I have played it together the most, and so far we have doubled the Glory Point goals just to have a longer experience. She has so far been underwhelmed by the endgame--you get your glory point goal and that's it, without any real feeling of accomplishment. Personally, given the randomness and luck factors, it's more of a pleasure to play as an aesthetic experience--colorful dice, colorful characters, fantasy element, and very light competition--there's not much you can really get aggressive about with other players. Still a fun experience for me, as I have enjoyed making the small decisions regarding capturing dice, summoning creatures, and comparing numbers. 

The only small caveat I have is that for how light the game is, it certainly seemed to take a while to grasp the mechanics. The cards are text-heavy, too, and each one throws a different element into reading the dice. Whether or not that's a drawback I suppose is up to individual players. For me, it adds to the social element of the game. Finding some play mats with the turn orders outlined has been a great help as well.

RATING: 6.5
Már egy ideje kinéztem magamnak ezt a játékot. Általában kettesben játszunk, és vágytam valami könnyedebb játékra a sok komoly között, ami viszonylag gyors, és nem tekeredik meg az agyad a végére. Ígyhát megvásároltam a Quarriors-t!. :)
Maga a doboz elég kicsike, de gyönyörű a kivitelezése. Szép masszív fémdoboz, ami egy dobókockát formáz. A tartalom minőségére se lehet sok panasz. A kártyák extra vastagok és nagyon jó minőségüek, a rajzok szépek. A dobókockák színe, és formája nagyon jó, azonban a rajtuk lévő feliratok néhol nagyon aprók, és ezen felül néhol a festés se teljesen tökéletes. Emiatt nehezen olvashatók.
Néhol dobókockás dominionként emlegetik a játékot, de messze nem igényel annyi gondolkodást és kombinálást, mint a kártyás nagytestvére. Az egész inkább kicsit olyan munchkinos könnyed szorakozás, valamennyi agyalással vegyítve. Mi kicsit változtattunk a szabályokon, mert két játékossal 20 pontig nagyon hamar el lehet érni ezért általában 40-ig játszunk. Így jobban fel lehet építeni a paklit (vagy hogy hívják ezt kockák esetén? :)). Ezen felül plusz egy varázslatot szoktunk még betenni, mert a varázslatok növelik igazán a kombinációs lehetőségeket.
A játékot még csak kétjátékos üzemmódban próbáltuk ki, remélem hamarosan összehozunk egy nagyobb partit is. Állítólag négyesben már nagyon nehéz pontot szerezni benne.
Bátran ajánlom mindenkinek ezt a játékot, aki könnyed, gyors, kicsit agyalós játékra vágyik. Az ára kicsit húzós a dobozban található komponensekhez viszonyítva, de a tartozékok és a doboz minősége kiegyenlíti az eltérést. :)

RATING: 5
[i]August 2011, First Impressions:[/i] Excellent rules-light dice pool-building game. My eight year old son really liked the game and asked to play another game right after the first (which is uncommon).

There's a lot of hype surrounding this game, but time will tell if the game is enjoyable enough to hit the game table.

The rulebook was easy to read and understand. We rarely referred to the rulebook after the first game.

The components were pretty good. I've heard some people complained about the quality of the cards but I don't see this as an issue as the cards don't get shuffled or handled a lot. Some of the dice were hard to read but the cards proved to be an adequate reference.  It was difficult to tell the black scoring marker from the blue one. These are all minor nits.

A rating of "6" (+1 for kids).

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
There's a runaway leader problem in this game.  The most important thing tends to be buy the super-expensive dice if ever you have the chance.  But the dragons and you'll win. The fix is to add a culling rule: you can only score glory (VP) for a die if you 'cull' it and put it back out on the board where someone else will have the chance to pick it up.  Otherwise you can continue to use it for its abilities, but not score from it.  This makes for a longer game but helps with the runaway leader problem. Of course, luck of purchasing high-valued dice aside, better players can make better decisions amongst what is left than a less experienced or thoughtful player.  I admit I played on my tablet for a while but my feelings about this game are pretty meh.

iOS: available, as well as one expansion

RATING: 7.5
Et deckbuilding-spil med terninger = En hurtigere og sjovere måde at spille deckbuilding-spil på! 

Mine erfaringer med spillet er endnu begrænsede, men min fornemmelse er, at ovenstående beskrivelse passer rigtig godt på [b]Quarriors![/b] Jeg har efterhånden prøvet rigtig mange forskellige deckbuilding-spil og det er efterhånden blevet en smule monotont. Og så dukker [b]Quarriors![/b] op som udskifter kortene med terningerne og dermed indfører den spænding som nogle elsker og andre snarere elsker at hade. RATING: 8.5
Fun and short.  You make your major decisions when you eye the cards at the beginning of the game and decide what, say, 2-4 dice you're going to combo.  From there, as they say, the die is cast.  You can make some minor adjustments to respond to your opponents dice but overall you're mostly playing out those early decisions.  It's so short a game that I think it works.

Yellow dice are way too inexpensive, though, total no brainer.  It's not game breaking or anything but as far as I can see it should be pretty scripted into the game that the first players buy portals if they can afford them.

Our variant to improve the game is quite simple: allow each player to buy 2 quarry die base instead of 1, no 2 buys of the same die in a turn.  Also, eliminate the end game condition of running out of dice on 4 creatures or whatever.  It turns the game into a much better deck building game, speeds the game up a bit, and provides harder choices during the buy phase.  With this variant the game improves immensely, I strongly recommend trying.  I've only tried it with 2, though I don't know what would be stopping it from work with 3 or 4.

Update: Whaddya know, the original rule was 2 buys.  I still don't play with the alt culling rule yet but may do so eventually.  Own 3 expansions, the game is good filler and couples play.

RATING: 7
The difference between deck-building and dice-building is that in deck-building you generally know what's going to happen when you draw the card. In dice-building, you have six sides with which to contend. This makes for a fairly frustrating game when someone wants to approach the game like Dominion. In Quarriors! you simply shrug and let the dice do their thing. My comment on Dominion is that an advanced player will undoubtedly defeat a newcomer with no way of balancing it. Quarriors! attempts to strike that balance, but it seems like it goes a bit too far in that while players have choices they are still just along for the ride. But the game is fun. I read somewhere that dice are popular for games because we like the tactile sensation and the minor use of motor skills...and it's true! And this is a great follow-up game for when people are frustrated by Dominion.

RATING: 7
[i]initial reaction[/i]
A dice based 'deckbuilding' game, in which players purchase dice to add to their pool. Q plays similarly to other games like Dominion, except that every hand you have to roll the dice you've drawn for that turn, so you never know quite how well your spells/creatures will perform on any given turn.

An interesting twist on the usual deckbuilding idea, but it adds another element of luck which may not suit everyone. I also found it a little confusing having some information on the dice and other information on the card, unlike the card games where everything you need is on the cards you have in your hand.

RATING: 7
It is increasingly clear to me that what I want out of a deck-builder is out of step with what most want; I do not enjoy Dominion or Ascension, but love Thunderstone and like Quarriors. This is despite their rather pointed lack of balance and their difference from each other. 
Allow me to enumerate Quarriors' faults; its aforementioned lack of balance, it can be over too fast, it can be too swingy (dice game, after all), lack of good cost spreads (not enough expensive cards), lack of many meaningful choices on a turn (not as bad as Dominion, but not as good as Thunderstone)...
But I don't care. Chucking colorful, attractive dice is fun, and while it can be too fast Quarriors rarely wears out its welcome.

It is a game i like and will play when others suggest it but not a game I am going to shell out the cash for.

RATING: 7

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Quarriors! is an excellent game that just missed my top 5 from this year. Dominion started a new genre of board game a few years back: deckbuilding. Quarriors! takes that concept and does it with dice instead of cards.

However, the differences it has from Dominion seem to be the things that sharply divide people. For example, Dominion is all about efficiency. You need to build an "engine" of cards that all support each other, get rid of the stuff in your deck that doesn't support it, and do so faster than anyone else. Quarriors!, by contrast, has less opportunity for getting rid of bad dice (and there are more bad dice to get rid of) and it is also much more about attacking other people. You are much more likely in this game to base your decisions around what other people are doing rather than trying to do the same thing better.

There are a few drawbacks, yes. I don't like the tin it comes in, the scoring track weaves around so that it is very easy to move to the wrong score if you aren't paying attention, and I wish it could support another player or two.

Even so, I see an incredible depth to the game, not to mention the sheer tactile fun of rolling all those dice. I think this game just gets better with more plays. You can do a successful swarm strategy, buying lots of guys that boost attack and defense of other units. You can go for a fast build-up of quiddity (the money you buy things with) to buy that huge dragon as soon as possible or even the die that scores you points directly. How many spells should you add? They are super-powerful, but creatures are so important both for culling (getting rid of dice) and attacking other players. I've seen builds with almost nothing but quick-scoring death dealers, I've seen builds with tons of tiny guys and portals to draw a ton of dice, I've seen builds with Witching Hags that keep recurring other Witching Hags and just won't die. This game has all the paths to victory, combos, and variety of Dominion (in its initial 25-card set) without all the boring min/maxing.

But, is it actually better than Dominion? I don't think so. It's just as fun, but there are times when it seems like a good roll or two early can decide the whole game. They are different enough to want to own both, but if I could only have one, I'd take... Rune Age.

RATING: 5.5

I've seen 3-player games were we'd like to stop the leader, but roll nothing useful to attack with, and the game ends with scores of 2-2-15, which gives the impression there is a runaway leader issue.

After several additional plays, I have increased my rating to 5.5, but the runaway leader issue is flagrant, especially when someone gets one of the more expensive die at the start (such as the dragon).

Would like to try one or two of the expansions to see if it resolves any of the above issues, else I don't see the rating going any higher.

RATING: 7
My first impression of Quarriors was pretty harsh, but it has definitely grown on me. I was annoyed at the luck of the dice piled on top of the luck of the draw, and concerned with the balance, or lack thereof. But to focus on those issues is to lose sight of the game's many charms. Deck building is fun, multicolored dice piles are fun, monster battles are fun. It's short, easy to understand, and quick moving. It's over before you could possibly get tired of it.

Yeah, it's lucky, and maybe the balance isn't quite right (still haven't decided on that one for sure), but it's clever, engaging, and fun. Quarriors offers something different, and it has earned a niche in my collection.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 4
11-28-11
There are parts of this game I thought I would like a lot.  I figured the dice aspect would be maybe a more "fun" version of Dominion, something light-hearted and easy to learn.  What I found was pretty much nothing but bad game here though, riding on hype.

To start with what I ended up liking by the end of the game, the artwork and different dice are nifty.  That's pretty much about it.

Ripping off Dominion almost to a T, a random seed of cards is placed out on the board.  Unlike Dominion, where cards are typically pretty easy to understand, there are strange iconography to learn on each card, and different effects that you have to learn as you go along.  Similarly to Ascension (which I also disliked), you are at a strong disadvantage playing your first time.  Unless you're going to study the cards for minutes, it's difficult to formulate a strategy.  Compare to Dominion where many cards are pretty simple, and those that aren't are easily understood by taking a few seconds to read it over.

Leading on to the notion of strategy though, is the extreme randomness.  The dice bag is similar to the draw deck of Dominion, and that's an interesting mechanic.  However, the dice being random means there really is just an illusion of strategy.  One of our players who isn't necessarily a "strategic thinker" picked dice by how pretty they looked rather than reading what they did.  The player sitting next to her just explained what her options were when she rolled her new dice.  Yet, she was one turn from winning.  This isn't evidence per-se, but it does point to what I feel - the dice can only be fair over the long-term.  Considering how short this game is (why you only play to 12 in a four player game, I don't know - more on this later), a hot streak of a couple turns can pretty much decide the game.

If you do take the time to read the cards and their effects, you might at least try to follow a plan.  But since you can only buy one die per turn, if you don't manage to roll a good side of the die when it comes up, your purchase is useless.  In Dominion, it may be random as to when you get your card, but when you get your card, you at least get the choice to use it!  Here though, all you want are creatures with high defense, like the Wizard.  The wizard dominated our game - no attacks (other than the extraordinarily expensive Dragon) were able to kill a wizard.  This was guaranteed scoring if you rolled the creature side of the die.  In Dominion, I've not yet seen a card other than perhaps the Chapel that will trump the entire set of cards.

Even if you get a great set of dice though, by the time  you're actually seeing it payoff, the game ends.  Twelve points is very short, and there is very little more than engine building.  This was a blessing for me, as I had no chance of winning after not choosing a Wizard (my mistake for not sitting to evaluate every card, and also for not rolling 7 money soon enough).  The game ended quickly, but felt ultimately pointless.  It's not light enough to feel fun like Killer Bunnies with the randomness that game has. The combat is there to piss each other off, and the game isn't deep enough to appeal to me either.

I would rather play just about any other dice game, and if Dominion was available, I would never, ever choose Quarriors over Dominion.  A shallow, over-hyped ripoff at best.
