RATING: 10
Deceptively simple, but there's a lot more to it than just dice chucking, so if you want to really get into it, take your time and read EVERYTHING (rules, monsters effects, spell, etc). You can learn playing it in minutes, but it takes way more to master it. A lot of the complaints probably come from ppl that either hate dice based games, or maybe they jump into it before properly digesting all available information. Yes, there will be times when a player will be able to score big and run with it, but more often than not, you can keep up the pace if you understand the best ways to pull out dice fast and how to constantly score. So you can play it at a very basic level, call it an "all luck dice chucking fest" and be done with it, or spend more time to learn all its tricks and then you may understand why people love this game so much. Very, very underrated gem!

RATING: 5.2
Aug11:

The novelty of course has to be tried!

As published, it just didn't work for me.  Multiplayer in particular was absolutely no fun.  2-player is bearable.

With the following house rules it was more fun for us:

1. When rolling you may move 1 active die to your used pile to re-roll another active die.

2. You may cull a die when one of your creatures is killed in combat; not when you kill a creature in combat.

July12:

Even with the second expansion, this game just couldn't make it.  It was fun for a bit, but the horrible balance of abilities just ruined it.  I like my games with variety *and* balance.  It requires more playtesting, but that's okay with me.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
The idea of a "dice-building" game sounds amazing! But then you start playing and begin to realise the randomness of rolling multitudes of dice will always overwhelm any potential for strategy.

Not only is your success based on your ability to effectively roll a handful of dice, but you're also hoping to randomly choose good combinations of dice from your bag. I can see exactly why others have called this  game 'randomness squared'! No matter what you do, the game will be won by the player who had the best dice rolls.

The game would probably benefit from being longer to help mitigate some luck and differentiate between players' different purchases. Also, it would help if there was some sort of alternate win condition so you can make more productive use of 'bad rolls'.

I can see myself playing this game from time to time if I'm in the mood for something super-light with dice and powers. Even then I'd probably rather play King of Tokyo...

(note, have only played 4 player)

RATING: 4
I don't usually rate games after one play, but in Quarrior's case I'll make an exception. Knowing going in that the game was going to be light for my tastes, I wanted to like it regardless. The concept was novel and portions of it are well-executed. But, the player who won our game literally made no tough decisions. He rolled well enough that he bought dragons on his first two turns, and he spent the rest of the game using them to win. It was very unsatisfying, both for him, and for the rest of us.

I have no doubt that it's possible to have fun games of Quarriors, but if experiences like mine are also possible, then the game is unfortunately flawed.

2012-03-02

Initial Rating: 7.0

At first glance this is a fine game, but there is something that bothers me about it. I love Dominion-like games and it is really nice that I don’t have to shuffle in Quarriors, however I don´t enjoy the game very much, it has more luck than I feel comfortable with for this kind of game, in Dominion you will always (almost always) have access to the powerful cards you have bought, in Quarriors you will draw the powerful creatures but you are never sure if you can trigger them or not. Almost no interesting decisions to make, either your roll was good or not.

At least it is fast, and that’s the only reason I will play it again, but it is also what bugs me the most, the game ending feels so abrupt I don´t like it at all, and like the game it is pure luck.

A game I will play again but not a game for me.

[hr]
2014-02-11

After more plays (thanks to the iPad) I like Quarriors less and less. You can cull as many dice as creatures earned glory this turn? This is just ridiculously  unbalanced, if you scored, it means you are getting ahead, and in reward they let you cull the trash from your “deck” making it much more likely to score again while your opponents draw the trash they still have.  I think an expansion changed the rule, but as this is from the base game, the rating needs to go down. This is an absurd rule.

Definitively, I won’t play Quarriors EVER again face to face, it is just an unbalanced luck fest. On the other hand, I think I can play it from time to time on the iPad as the game is much more quickly. The art and the dice are gorgeous, but this is the only thing the game has for it.

RATING: 4
It's a decent light weight game, but there really is no strategy in it at all for me. I want to like this more than I do, and I do enjoy playing it as a filler, but I really feel like the rules need some fix. There is almost no catch up, no way to make your guys survive, and I've seen to many early leaders win out.

It's OK. Just that. 

Kevin Wilson suggested a fix for it that sounded like it may work. I wish to God, I could recall more than the first part of his tweak.

This may be saved by the next expansion, but for now, there's not much here and that makes me sad. I do have fun teaching people it, but it is not lighting me up...I like the dice though. I think that's cool...and the potential for 6 variations on a card.

RATING: 7.5
Two plays only, but I'm liking this.

'Dominion-dice' seems a popular label, but I find this a lot more fun. Weed your deck, buy strong, hope the right dice come out at the right time - and roll lucky! There's more interaction, 'though - you attack the others and must anticipate them attacking you, and your tactical decisions are in part based on the others' scores and what dice they have in play.

Only gripe: playability would really have benefitted from the text being bigger on both the cards and the dice. 'What's the benefit of that spell again? I can't read the card from here'.

Overall, a lot of fun.

This dynamic creates and odd dichotomy that makes people either love or hate this game.  Where the game truly shines is it's fast game play (quicker than Dominion ... usually and much  quicker than Thunderstone), the novelty of dice-building, and it's simple rules.  In short, this game is easier to get to the table than a lot of other games I own. 

I know my rating will go up from 7.5

RATING: 8
So much better than it is given credit for. They remade quarriors into dice masters which gets a lot of love for good reason. The biggest problem with the game was the horrible decision in the original rules to only allow one purchase per turn and not culling on scoring. If you don't play with the advanced rules, you aren't playing it right. Game would score a 5 with the basic rules and is why this has been held back by the community (reviewers like the dice tower, etc still really like it). Expansions add something to the mix, but the base set is a great family game. It is much easier to teach than you would expect. It really is a family game.

RATING: 3

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
In Quarriors, each player plays as a Quarrior competing for Glory in the eyes of Quiana, the Empress of Quaridia. Each player starts with an identical set of 12 dice in their dice bag. At the start of the game, Creature and Spell dice (Quarry) are dealt at random to form "the Wilds" in the center of the table. As play progresses, players roll their dice to attempt to summon Creatures, cast Spells, and harness the magical power of Quiddity (the in-game resource) to capture Quarry from the Wilds to add to their repertoire and into their dice bag. Players draw and roll 6 dice a turn from their bag, making that controlling bag composition is key to victory.Quarriors is a fast-paced game where players must strategically balance their choices each turn. Do I use my Quiddity to summon Creatures in the hopes of scoring Glory or should I spend it all to capture more powerful Quarry from the Wilds? Players must outmaneuver their opponent's through strategic Spell use, the acquisition of powerful Quarry, and ultimately, by striking down opponent's Creature in combat. If your Creatures survive until your next turn, you will score Glory points and move closer to victory!

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 4
The idea behind this game is very interesting, but the implementation is not that good. It's basically a deck building game with dice. Each dice is represented by a card to know the rules of the die. The cycling of the dice is exactly the same as a deck building game.

The only good idea I wound in this game is how targeting and scoring is done. You summon creatures that are going to attack all players at the same time, but on your next turn, you discard these monsters and get victory points for them. So you only temporarily build up your army.

The problem with this game is that there is almost no decision. You don't have a choice to re-roll dice like it is the case in dune express and to court the king. You also have the problem of double randomness since the dice are also draw randomly. So it ends in a pure random fest. I would suggest to add a bit more strategy that the players could select the dice they want to roll from their bags.

Second, I think the game ends really short. You have little time to build your deck (dice pool). Spells are also not that interesting to buy because you actually need creatures to be supported by spells. So spells alone are useless. But the worst is that you cannot split you buying power on 2 dice, like buy a weak creature and a spell, you can only buy 1 die at a time. So the progress is just really slow and the game ends before you know it.

RATING: N/A

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
This is essentially a dice-building game of the deck-building genre (think of it like "Dominion meets fantasy slugfest").  Instead of cards used to build decks, it is dice used to build "decks" (or pools of chance).  Players duel each other with the warriors and spells that they buy and roll.  A warrior die, half the time, rolled usually generates a ranked warrior type (maybe with special features), or the other half rolled, usually generates money.  Money is used to activate a warrior rolled or pooled to purchase another die.  Quick, light fun dice-generating game.

RATING: 9
I have played this game a few times, maybe fifteen or so games, two plays a sitting, and I love this game. At first I was disappointed by the sheer amount of dice and cards and various things to figure out but once that I over this game is fun. What makes Quarriors so interesting is the use of dice instead of cards and the use of community cards. Every person has the same availability as everyone else, it's just a matter of rolling the required amounts at the right time to get them. Far too many people call this "luck" but it really isn't. If I have the opportunity to grab a creature but opt not to it isn't luck when I don't get that creature. If I need more resources (quiddity) I can get a portal to try to get more, cull a dice to better my chances of drawing quiddity, re-roll creatures for their quiddity sides, or look for spells that offer a combination of those choices. Selection is important. Yes, i have rolled hands of pure quiddity when I should have rolled creatures, and I have rolled more creatures than I had quiddity. Perfect hands, not so perfect outcomes. That's just the game, isn't it? It is exhilarating when an opponent doesn't get their creatures, and it hurts when you don't get yours, but it all evens out. Over games the person with the best hand, strategy, and luck wins. Take chances, get creatures, try to beat their best creatures with killing spells. The best part about Quarriors is the cards. Every dice has three, sometimes four cards, all with VARIANTS of the same theme, so the game is always different, the choices different. This is a must-have game. I played with two, three, and four players, but three seems to be just about perfect. With two if you have a creature it will probably score, with three it might, with four it won't. My problem with the base game is that the highest cost creatures (dragons and wizards) usually win the game, and the dice get beat up, chipping the paint. Otherwise this game is perfect.

RATING: 6
Sure there's a lot of luck in the dice and quite a bit of downtime to start as you read what all the dice do, but the former is also true with a lot of newer deck builders and those also often have a lot of downtime reading as new cards come out in their fluctuating buy row. Also, newer deck builders often have a rich get richer syndrome, where in Quarriors you have to lose your dice when you score. Factor in the short playing time and decent amount of interaction and I found this quite enjoyable.

I also like this more than its supposed successor Dice Masters, as I didn't find Dice Masters' combat system to make thematic sense, and I prefer a new puzzle of which dice are optimal each game, rather than making the majority of my dicebuilding decisions beforehand and then going through the motions again in the actual game.

RATING: 6
At its best it's really satisfying to collect colorful dice and build a strong strategy. At its worst, a winner is apparent in the first 15 minutes and it drags on for a miserably long time, or somebody with a solid dice bag gets completely screwed by their rolls *in addition to* their draw RNG. In general a solid purchase if you like colorful dice, or have players who like them. :)

The player mats not being included was surprising and annoying. The fan-made ones I found on BGG were easier to understand and more attractive than the mat in the rule book.

The rule book is also not very good. It is one of those where nothing makes sense until you have read and memorized every single statement and reorganized them all into a more logical order in your head. It completely fails to explain the basic overall concepts and "shape" of the game, so that there is no reference point from which to understand all the details it immediately whacks you with. You can also find yourself in slightly fuzzy situations that you have to read the rules closely to resolve (if at all). It is trivial to explain the rules to new players much more simply.

Most of the confusion results from the exact timings of when dice are moved between areas, when they can be used, or whether they can be saved for later, but no overall "rule" or chart is provided to help understand the timings clearly.

RATING: 6
Quarriors is a fresh take on the typical deck-building formula. Using bags of dice instead of cards makes shuffling your "deck" much less of a hassle, and the characters and spells are very charming. The game is really exiting to play and there's a special drama to dice rolling that makes the game very tense, but my group has come to find that whoever is winning within the first 10 minutes ends up taking the whole game. I don't think we've had a single play-through where this wan't the case. Also, I have the version that comes in the tin. While is a very nice little collector's item, it's not very practical as a storage device. The cards won't fit in their slot once sleeved, and you're pretty much forced to use those teeny tiny awful zip-lock bags. You're better off coming up with your own storage solution.

Quarriors has the frenetic excitement of a dice battle game, with an added ‘deckbuilding’ twist: players customize their dice pools during the game using resources generated by their rolls.
Quarriors takes the best of deckbuilding games without the tedium of shuffling. Take a typical deckbuilding game, add the speed and fun of dice and in 60 minutes you’re on your second or third game trying unique strategies against your opponents.

RATING: 7
Now this was a pleasant surprise! I was wondering how Eric Lang, one of my favorite designers, could have gone and produced something as shallow and unbalanced as many critiques of Quarriors implied, but after a couple of rounds found that I needn’t have worried: this is not only a nice idea in expensive clothing - garish, also - but a fully functioning and funny game with quite a bit of strategy crammed into the 20-30 minutes a normal round should last. Do I buy creatures or spells? Do I aim for a steady increase of victory points or start with building up my quiddity reservoir in the hopes of catching up with big creatures later?

Of course, there is a healthy dose of luck involved, but if you are capable of being disappointed by unexpected randomness in a game that clearly involves random rolls you perform with a random set of dice, then I can’t help you. A less inane line of criticism is targeted at the very big creatures and the unfair advantages they give to a player who manages to capture one of them early in the game. These biggies, especially the dragon, are pretty impressive, but in the four or fives games I’ve played, they did not turn out to lead to guaranteed wins.

The only thing that kept me from instantly going out and buying this after my first few games is the attempted cheek-in-tongue tone of the rules and the cartoonish graphics. While this can be pulled of, especially if you’re Vlaada Chvátil, it can also come across as rather juvenile. Nevertheless, the sheer fun of Quarriors is probably enticing enough to make me buy this, garish graphics be damned.

The dice look great (a bit hard to read, but I understand the cost/size tradeoff), and they're fun to roll. I like that the different versions of each creature are substantially, game-changing-ly different, not just cosmetically so. There's enough strategy to hold my interest, but not so much that I feel like I should play Dominion instead -- it fills its own niche.

As cool as the giant cube tin is, I fucking hate storing this thing.

RATING: 8.5
Played 2 2-player games, 2 3-player games, 1 4-player game

The game is very easy to teach especially if the players have had experience with Dominion or Magic: The Gathering.

I introduced it to a buyer for a local hobby store last night and he hadn't stocked it yet because he didn't see it selling.  After one play he is now going to stock it.

So far, I like the game.  It's plays fast, has tension, and it has a lot of fun/funny moments like when you pull exactly what you need from your bag but roll all the wrong combinations or when you are rooting for another player so he can stop someone else from winning.

That said, it also has it's downside.  It's dice so the randomness rules, but you are able to mitigate it by the dice pool you create.  As far as design is concerned, a few of the creatures are not balanced well and some of the spells can have effects that are useless depending on the cards you draw to play with.

This is not a hardcore strategy game.  Its for those who like a fun light-hearted game with friends that doesn't last all night.

RATING: 7
To a certain extent Dominion with dice where dice are bought instead of cards, except for that (1) the dice bought can be "scored" and are returned to the bank, and (2) active dice can cancel out the opponents' active dice. In all, Quarriors! address all Dominion's shortcomings: the more direct interaction reduces Dominion's MP-solitaire nature; using dice in a baf alleviates the tedium of Dominion's incessant shuffle-fest; and the lack of theme cohesion Dominion suffers is avoided by the very form of the game. Recommanded for fans of fast deck-building games.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
ARGH!  This game is the epitome of the difference between a great IDEA and a great GAME.  When I first heard about Quarriors, I thought it was brilliant: capitalize on the deck-building fad by building dice, rather than cards.  And the potential for an incredible game is hidden somewhere, but I certainly couldn't find it anywhere in this box.  I feel that in a good deck-building game, you start with a few meager resources and you begin by trying to acquire more with more frequency.  You get progressively stronger with each phase of the game, and you eventually transition from a resource engine to a victory point engine.  In Quarriors, that sequence doesn't exist.  The best dice are the best in every way: strength, defense, victory points, extra powers.  Their only downside is their cost, but you can purchase every die available at the very beginning of the game.  In fact, you can never gain more buying power than the dice you have in your opening hand!  I'm so disappointed with how great this game could have been versus how mediocre it turned out to be.  My rating is far more harsh than this game really deserves, but every time I play, I can't get over how much better I wish it was.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5.5
Game in a Nutshell: "Deck-building" game of dice where you're trying to roll monsters with better then your neighbors. If your monsters survive a trip around the table, they score points.

Played as: 4 player

Pros: Has it's own unique spin on a "deck-building" game. It's a lot easier to pull dice out of a bag, then constantly shuffling cards. Any game with this many pretty dice has pizazz and is going to draw attention from any passers by. Has that satisfying feeling of throwing a handful of heavy plastic.

Cons: It's not as accessible of a game as you would expect for something as light as it is. I didn't like how the abilities of your dice are listed in small text on a card that's central to everyone. Leaning and squinting over text is not at all relaxing. Somehow lacks that tension/excitement that dice usually bring to whatever game they are part of. Expensive, and unfortunately you're paying up for the bunch of custom, sparkly dice rather than a particularly stellar design with lots of mileage.

Other: My overall impression of it was it's ok, but not as good of a "deck-building" game as Dominion and not as good as a dice game as Roll Through the Ages. The biggest killer is you could buy both those games combined for about the same price as just Quarriors. That alone makes it a tough game to justice my recommendation.

Best Use: Probably not bad for a casual time after players get a couple games under their belts.

RATING: 7

As it stands, there is a LOT of game with all the expansions, in a pretty small box, so I think I'll have this one for a long time.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 3
It's fun enough, but it has way too quick of a "ramping up" period. You can effectively buy the best "card" in the game on your very first turn if your roll even marginally well. There's no real feeling of "deck" development or progression. Costs of "summoning" the units are also way too low, which means you don't really have to give up a lot to get your army going.

The game clearly isn't designed to present players with difficult decisions, but rather to just let them have some fun rolling dice and beating each other over the head with fantasy creatures with cool super-powers. I bought the super-awesomest unit in the game on my first roll and pretty much sailed on to victory [no other unit in the game could kill him!].

Furthermore, the game would be a lot more fun if the dice were bigger and/or you had a card in front of you summarizing the dice you had bought. Yes, it [i]works[/i], but in a game where "neat looking dice" is one of the chief appeals [possibly the only appeal] to have them so small and have so little stuff on the table is less than impressive.

RATING: 4.25
Amalgame bien le hasard de la pige au hasard de la valeur de ce qui est pigé. Du hasard au carré!

Pas vraiment de choix: on acquiert généralement ce qui vaut le plus cher en fonction des "manas" disponibles. De plus, la grande majorité des créatures offrent 50% de chance d'attaquer, et 50% de produire du "mana". Il manque le côté "gambling/conservateur" qu'offriraient par exemple certaines créatures qui le sont réellement sur cinq, voire les six faces du dé, versus une créature très puissante, mais sur une seule face.

Pas vraiment de stratégie: on semble pouvoir prendre les meilleures décisions et perdre malgré tout. On brasse et rebrasse, on fait un peu n'importe quoi, et puis ça marche ou pas et finalement hop, quelqu'un a gagné et c'est fini.

Le concept pouvait sembler prometteur. Mais dans l'application, je ne vois ni fun, ni intérêt à ce jeu.

NOTE: La règle semble mal écrite et manquer d'explications supplémataires concernant chaque créatures. Les "mats" à découper, pas l'idée du siècle non plus pour un jeu de ce prix.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
I LOVE dice. Love 'em.

Quarriors is a game that heavily requires a lot of luck to win at. You can choose which creatures to invest in, but all-in-all, if you don't roll them to be summoned, they're useless to you. 

You're forced to attack as soon as you summon a creature and it'd be interesting to allow players to keep them out on the board to attack as they wish before scoring them along the Glory Track. That would require an overhaul of the game, which will not happen.

A few expansions exist and they don't seem to add too much by way of game-changing experience as they're typically just more creatures and a few new spells. 

It's a fun game, just not terribly deep or strategic, which can kill its replay value. If you love the luck of the roll, give this one a shot.

RATING: 7
A deck building game with a very big twist. You're all building the same deck and the luck of the roll plays a vital but non-critical role. If you roll your die right, you'll have a big advantage, but building up the deck properly requires a fair amount of thought. Once everyone gets a hang of the rules, it's a very quick game that can take on wide range of forms, and it can also be a ton of fun even if you only have two people.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Quarriors is a "dice building" game very similar to Dominion in that you are progressively building a collection of dice.  The scoring mechanic is the biggest difference.  Rather than rolling scoring dice, creature dice are rolled which, if they survive the round, then score victory points.  After several games I've found that I like Quarriors just fine, but it isn't one of my top favorites.  I'm glad to have it on hand as it is easy to explain and a great gateway game.  So much luck is involved, however, that I can't say that I will often recommend this to my hardcore group.  
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 3

RATING: 3
Quarriors! takes the resource pool and starting deck ideas from deckbuilding games like Dominion and converts them to dice which sextuples the possibilities of what each draw and purchase can become beyond just the order of how the player's 'deck' is 'shuffled'. The shuffling that would occur in a regular deck-builder is streamlined here as well; you just gather up and roll the dice. Easy!

The negative side of reducing most of the gaming components to dice is the table is literally empty. There is no eye candy and combat is reduced to numbers with nothing to prime players' imaginations.

After a few plays we have discovered that some of the dice suck, no matter which version of description card gets pulled.

RATING: 6.5
It has many virtues: fun, fast, fresh, easy to teach... 

But it has one big problem: it's not balanced. Some creatures are almost worthless (even the expensive ones) and some others are great (great defense that allow to score them easily + other creatures you get in the same roll, etc.). That limits very much the winning strategies and it's the diference between the good game it is and the great, great game it would be if balanced.

Depending on the number of players, there are always some dice that are better than others. By far, the most balanced games are 1vs1. The game works very well on those games.

A minor problem (solved with expert culling rules): most games finish too fast, so:
· long term strategies not worthy
· at midgame, you limit the number of dice you can buy even more: you can only take the ones that provide short term benefits

Anyways, luck is heavily involved and these issues do not kill the game. When played fast, the game is still fun to play.

The dice quality is questionable.

There are a couple of house rules that really improve the game:
- You can buy 2 dice per turn, not just one.
- You have to cull the creature dice you score with.

These house rules increase the value of minor dice and spells, and contributes to see much more varied strategies. After almost 150 games, I see these house rules make a huge difference.

Also, the Quartifacts expansion adds a the missions mechanic that really add a new level of depth to the game it didn't have before. I don't want to play Quartifacts without the expansion anymore, except, maybe 2 player games. I still have to try the expert culling house rules with it.

RATING: 7.95
A lot of dice! Dominion on dice! This is an addictive game. You begin the game with a base set of dice and a bag. And like Dominion there will be descriptive cards out for you to purchase...wait...not purchase cards...but purchase dice off from the cards. So you go to the Quarry (or village in Dominion) and purchase the dice you will need. Similar to Dominion (when you build your deck with the cards you purchase)you will be collecting dice to build your bag of dice. So simple. 

The game is quick to play maybe a bit faster than Dominion. The dice battle your opponents dice with their unique special powers all notated on the dice. Fun and strategic game! The rulebook however, is a little convoluted with annoying Q's.

RATING: 7
*放之三種基礎卡片，兩顆assitant 骰跟5顆portal骰，生物卡洗勻，一直開到有7種不同生物(重複的就棄掉)，咒語卡一樣開到三種，拿對應的5顆骰放在這些卡上
*每個玩家拿一個計分標記放0分，一個袋子跟圖版，8顆quiddity骰和4 assistant骰，丟進袋中，隨機起始
*quiddity是貨幣，可招換生物或買骰，重骰骰面可馬上重骰該骰，抽骰的則該骰放到啟動區來抽骰，生物咒語面則看要不要使用從啟動區移動到準備區，最左邊是已使用區為使用過的骰子，怪物骰左上等級右上攻擊右下防禦左下是否爆發
*卡片左上是花費，右上是存活分
*遊戲進行到有人達到指定分數(2/3/4:20/15/12)或是有四種生物骰被買完馬上結束，每回合有六個階段
1.準備區生物拿分，移到使用區，每一個移動的骰子可讓你移除使用區的骰子回中央區
2.抽六顆骰放啟動區，在一起擲，如果不夠抽，先抽完在把使用區丟進去，立即效果要馬上使用(抽骰，重擲)
3.咒語面移動到準備區，怪物面則要花費左上金額移到準備區
4.所以準備區怪打所有其他人的怪，加總你的攻擊力減去其他人防禦力，傷害分配由防守方自由分配，由左手玩家開始結算
5.花錢買最多一顆骰放到已使用區(可不做)
6.所有啟動區骰移到使用區，準備區咒語也可移到使用區
*只有準備區的生物面骰才算是生物
*附加咒語一定要放在生物上，生物掛也消失，反應咒語可在別人回合放

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
Quarriors is like Dominion but not as good. I only played 2 games so didn't see everything, but Dominion has quite a few game changer cards that are very different to the vanilla "+1X +2Y's" style cards and turn the game upside down and inside out. Chapel, Gardens, Witch spring to mind.

Quarrior dice felt like lots of flavours of vanilla - the expensive dice were mostly scaled up versions of the cheaper dice. Dice! Yes you roll them, so it's like a card with 6 possible states, though typically a dice will either give you:
A. money (called 'Quiddity' which I rather like) or
B. it's power (thematically one of your minions or a spell you are casting). Some of the dice have a third kind of side that will be 'A/B' or allow a re-roll of another dice.
So sometimes you'd rather have A but roll B and vice versa. Yes, sometimes you can re-roll them, depending on which dice you buy, but then you have to roll the re-roll, and the re-roll dice itself is taking up one space of your hand size, so I am pretty confident there is more luck in Quarriors.

The way you score VPs is different to Dominion, imagine instead of buying a Duchy and that Duchy being a dead card, you play a Duchy from your hand and then if it gets back round to you with your Duchy still there, you score 3 VPs and discard the Duchy. When you play your Duchy it immediately attacks all other peoples VP cards - that's how you might not score. Again this will come down to luck - if you have a big scoring monster out you either will or will not score for it, depending on whether your opponents managed to roll their monster or if they rolled money. Rolling money is bad in the late game, because you can't directly buy VPs in a can. Dominions scoring with 'dead cards' was better IMO, ingenious even. I guess the gimmick, the main appeal of Quarriors (dice rolling) is it's main flaw. Perhaps it is silly to play a game made of dice and complain about the luck.

RATING: 6
An interesting take on the deck-building genre, using dice instead of cards, but they've made something so devoid of decision making and so luck bound that, although it was an ok pastime, it didn't engage me at all. Your starting dice have gold on them, you roll them, see how much gold you've earned, and buy a new dice from the 10 different types of dice on offer. Add it to your discard pile. When eventually you get through your dice, add your discard pile back into your bag, draw 6 more and so on - same as a deck. When eventually you get to roll your newly bought dice, it could come up with more gold (frustrating) but hopefully will roll up a creature or spell. Creatures have an attack number and a defense number, so the total value of the creatures you deploy on your turn attacks each other player in turn, killing off creatures with lower defense. If your creatures can survive until your next turn, then you score VPs. The problem is you can study all the creature and spell powers, buy good combinations, buy the best you can, and then never have the dice you want in the same roll together, or the creature dice don't actually roll on the creature side so you can't earn VPs. And after the initial accumulation moves, it just takes a few good rolls by one player to end the game quite quickly before you get a chance to really use and re-use stuff. There's little or no dice manipulation, so the decisions to make on your turn are what to re-roll if you get a re-roll (trivial), and then what to buy with your money, which might seem interesting at first blush, but turns out to be rather less consequential than you'd hope given you'll only see each dice a few times and on half of those nothing will happen. Btw downtime is pure ... there's nothing to do on other player's turns except study what you might want to buy next turn. All up, less than it might have been.

RATING: 4
What I liked:
- Rolling dice is generally fun
- Shaking dice in a bag is more fun than shuffling cards
- Despite complaints that there are no decisions in the game, I found there were some
- All the cards probably give the game replayability

What I didn't like:
- Our 4p game took almost 2 hours, and we weren't going slow
- The dice are hard to read (fine print, poor colors)
- The cards are hard to read (fine print, center of table) and/or memorize
- Some leader-bashing (via spells)
- I don't like games with direct attacking, although I appreciate that most of the attacks in this game are not directed at individuals (but see spells above)

Overall, not a game I would want to play again. I might be interested in a Dominion-like dice-based deck-building game (not based on attacks), but the reliance on those cards in the center of the table is a real negative for me. I did cull dice, but didn't have the same deck-building feel that I get with Dominion.

If the game really lasted 15-30 minutes, it would be far more appealing. Maybe we just got a *really* bad set of cards in the quarry, but it didn't seem like a bad mix, with cards of various values, and with both attacks and defenses.

The weight of 1.9 seems about right, with the sometimes-complex special abilities of the different creatures and spells.

- Instead of scoring the creature and moving it to the used pile, keep it and do not score.
- You may capture up to 2 dice from the wilds on your turn (as long as you can pay for both, obviously).
- Dragon grants only 3 glory points (this one is a most in my opinion).

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Quarriors delivers a highly imperfect experiential version of its promised concept.  

The concept: drafted armies magically summoned and fighting one another, accruing victory points for surviving a full round of onslaught.

The mechanic: drafted dice that when rolled either give energy to summon or represent the monster to be summoned.

The flaw: You must do the following 1)Roll dice and hope for high energy to purchase good dice 2) hope to pull a good collection of dice randomly from your bag 3) roll the dice you've purchased and hope that they actually deliver on their highest potential.

If anything goes wrong in the above chain, the game almost instantly creates a massive disparity in success with momentum spiking insurmountably high after only 3-4 turns. Doesn't happen every time, but many games end in such a way.  

Thus the game is hard to recommend as anything other than a light bit of randomness, meant to not be taken seriously.  As such it can be fun.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Really like this game. It's much easier to learn if you're familiar with Dominion since they follow a similar principle. My complaints are that the terminology is a little confusing and could be done better and that the dice are so small that you lose some important detail (like small asterisks on only one or two sides are hard to see, or small numbers.) Biggest thing to watch out for is remembering to check which side of the die your monster has landed on and to check it's card because it's easy to forget nuances to the monsters. Might also be nice if there were 5 cards per monster, one to leave in the middle, one for each player as they purchase a die so that you don't have 4 people straining to read all the cards in the middle constantly.
Overall, great fun.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.9
Brilliant game, focusing and what's board gaming is about - having fun with something you can touch and feel. Refreshing approach to deckbuilding. Nothing beats that nice feeling when you shake your bag with dice before drawing. If you like dice, you should get this game, it's an absolute blast. It takes no more than hour, works great with 2 as well as with 4.

Ignore all those comments about "total randomness". It's as much random as dice can be, but there's quite a lot of decisions to be made. I don't know if people realize that there's huge chunk of math and probability being involved here and that you in fact have good amount of control over the content of your bag and thus the overall chance of coming out with desired outcome. If you pack lots of monsters, the chance you will roll more monsters increases. If you load your bag with quiddity, you will definitely buy some good dice soon, etc, etc. Of course, you can get a perfect set of dice, yet still be unfortunate with your roll, but a) it can STILL be mitigated by dice that allow you to re-roll! b) it just sometimes happens. That's the charm of board game, random weird situations happen, it would be a boringfest if you would just win all the time. But it's rarely a total screwage that will prevent you from winning the game. There's lot of suspense and tension, first when drawing your dice, second, when the actual roll happens. 

Depending on what spells and monsters are on the table, you can go different ways - build your strategy around lots of dice, attached spells, offensive spells, defensive creature abilities, side-effect creature abilities, lots of cheap creatures, one-two expensive creatures... geez, it's really a lot. One of the best games I've played.

Serious drawback is the dice quality, though. It's less than stellar. You'll find at least few of your dice to be borked in a certain way. You can replace some of them, but even then it's not the end of the world, as all the dice faces are repeated on cards, or you can usually just take another dice from table. Still, I don't like it. If you spend your $$$ on game, it should be perfect.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Initially I was very taken with Quarriors.  It was different to all the usual deck-building games, promised lots of variety and felt fresh.  But the more I played it, the more I fell out of love with it. 

For one thing, it started to feel more and more like a beta release; the rules were significantly altered with one of the early expansions when the designers realised that the game wasn't working properly.  This was confirmed as more  expansions were released and the system trotted out for other, similar games.

More significantly perhaps was my growing realisation that the game offered very few interesting decision points.  Buying dice during your turn?  Get the best you can.  Putting dice into play?  Obvious.  Attacking and blocking?  The same.

I'd stopped playing it before Marvel Dice Masters was released, but almost straight away it was clear to me that MDM was by far the better game, with many, many more meaningful decisions to be made.  Shame it's not multiplayer.

-  Aren't these two problems enough?

RATING: 4
Fun to try out, but I don't see any improvements over Dominion.  I understand the expansions are supposed to introduced more combos/synergy that the base game seems to be lacking, but as I've already invested in several Dominion expansions, I don't see myself investing in Quarriors+expansions.  Quarriors lacks the critical Dominion mechanic where as you buy victory points, you reduce your ability to buy more in the future; in Quarriors, buying big monsters is always good - they increase your purchasing power (usually have 2 or more faces with $2), have strong attacks to stop opponents from scoring points, and have strong defense and the highest victory point values. And of course, since you're rolling dice, you can get screwed by chance if you just fail to roll monster faces on your monster dice to summon them, which means you ar both unable to stop your opponents from scoring points and cannot score points yourself.  Lastly, in theory it should be more fun to shake dice in a bag and randomly draw them than to constantly shuffle cards, in practice constantly having to take exactly 6 dice out of the bag, or taking the last 2 and then putting the rest in the bag to shake and take 4, is more fiddly.  This is compounded by having dice that trigger off what dice opponents have in their used area, meaning players have to wait until their turn before refilling their bag, unlike Dominion where players can shuffle their discard pile during opponents' turns.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.4
Two Player Value = Good, but more competitive with more players.

Three Player Value = Good. 


Quarriors could be the ultimate dice game.  By that I mean it uses more dice then any other game ever.

Quarriors is a competitive deck pooling game that uses activated abilities of a die when rolled.  I think it is reasonable to say that most people will understand when I say it uses the Dominion deck pooling mechanic.  The difference here is that it uses dice, not cards, which is rather ingenious.  A six-sided die allows for six possible outcomes which means more variability in the game.  This is a fun idea.

Each turn the player draws and rolls six dice.  Since each side of each die may be different the player will have access to different activated abilities with each role.  This will further change with using different dice on each role for huge variation possibilities.

The deck pooling is handled nicely by using bags to contain the dice and draw dice from.

Quarriors is easy to learn and play making it a good choice for beginners or people new to gaming.

The interaction between the players is minimal as the players only affect each other during a single phase each turn.  This phase is used to attack the other player to prevent him from scoring.

The components of the game need to be noted.  Since the game is 98% dice I think good dice are important.  This is where Quarriors comes up short.  Many of the dice are not printed well.  The dice have poor moldings or the paint is missing in parts.  This makes those dice hard to read.  It is not done well and that is a shame.

The theme is really not integral to the game at all.  In fact, the game could work using just about any other theme imaginable.  However, I do not think that is a problem unique to this game, but rather a problem with the deck pooling mechanic in general.


Pros - 
- Tons of custom dice
- Fun to play
- easy to learn and good to beginners

Cons-
- Many poor quality dice 

Overall Playability = 7.2

Design = 8
Strategy = 7
Luck = 8
Artwork & Components = 4
Use of Mechanics = 8
Time of Play = 8
Fun = 9
Innovation = 7
Analysis Paralysis = 8
Theme = 5


***Rating includes playing with all the expansions


[b]-------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------[/b]


Here I will discuss the Quarriors expansion [thing=115098][/thing].

Overall I like this expansion and the additions it brings to the game.  

It does not change the base in any way other then adding new creatures and spells plus their associated dice.  But for this game I think that is a good thing.  The new dice help ramp up the variation and breath new life into the game.

One of the largest additions you gain with this expansion is the box.  The box for this expansion is large enough to hold this expansion, the base game and the Demons expansion.  It also has a very useful insert and cover that holds all the dice in place.  I truly loath most box inserts, but find this one to be fantastic in every way and is a big plus.

I will rate this expansion the same as the base game as it does not change the base game.  Therefore this expansion will carry the same rating as the base game.


[b]-------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------[/b]


Here I will discuss the expansion [thing=106895][/thing].

For a small expansion Rise of the Demons does a fair amount to alter the base game.  I like the way it adds to the base.

This expansion brings a new type of dice into the game which is cursed and bad for the player to own.  This is a fun new mechanic and works to choke the players draw bag.  Players will need to find a way to deal with cursed dice.

These cursed dice are given to players by their opponents by using new cursed versions of each character from the base game.  These new cursed characters are accompanied by a new character Demon Lord character which is extremely powerful.

Playing with a few of these cards is great way to mix-up the game.

With this expansion I will rate the base game one tenth higher.  I think this makes the strategy of the game that little bit better.  This will bring the game to - 

Overall Playability = 7.3


[b]-------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------[/b]


Here I will discuss the expansion [thing=103610][/thing].

Thes expansion does very little to add to the game.

This is simply three overpowered versions of base game characters.  They are fun to play with, but do little else.

This does not change the rating of the game.


[b]-------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------[/b]


Here I will discuss the Quarriors expansion [thing=128709][/thing].

At this point in the expansion history of Quarriors, the expansions feel fairly set, excepting the two smaller ones.  Each contains the same number of creatures, spells and dice. 

These six new creatures bring one small addition to the game, the ability to lock a die.  While this is neither a new mechanic, nor greatly used in this expansion, it does add just enough to change the game in a positive way.

This small positive chance is mostly due to the fact that each lockable die has a beneficial lock ability and a detrimental lock ability.  This makes for more strategic decision when choosing dice.

Also of note is the fact that the quality and appearance of the dice have improved.  The dice are much better in this expansion.

While I do like this small change and the expansion, it would only alter my overall rating of the game by one tenth most due to the better quality dice.


Overall Playability = 7.4


[b]-------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------[/b]


Here I will discuss the Quarriors expansion [thing=137496][/thing].

This is where I think the game begins to take an odd turn as the designers try to add to expansion formula.

In this expansion the normal formula is kept by adding the same number of creatures and spells as prior.  In addition a new mechanic is added; the ability to go on quests to possibly find powerful artifacts.

While I understand the desire to add new mechanics to the game so to expand  what actions the players have available to them, I find the new questing mechanic clunky and odd.  

The game is played normally.  However, now when players roll creatures they have the option of sending them to the quest card instead of just attacking their opponent.  Creatures at the quest card battle in the normal way.  Instead of scoring creatures at the beginning of the turn, those creatures now complete the quest.  Completed quests yield bonuses for the player.

I like the idea of the quests, but in practice they are rather useless.

I do like the new creatures and the quality of the dice is still very good.

This expansion does not change my overall score for this game.


[b]-------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------[/b]

RATING: 9
Quarriors! is a game of rolling dice in order to ready creatures for glory, smiting your fellow player's creatures, and recruiting more creatures into your fold.  Taking inspiration from the deck building genre, each player starts the game with an identical bag of 12 dice.  On his turn a player will score glory for any creature he still has in play from his previous turn, then he will randomly draw 6 dice from his bag and roll them.  Each die belongs to a set of dice, easily identified by color, and each set of dice has a corresponding card in the play area describing what each face of the die means.  A die is either a spell, a creature, or currency.  After rolling the dice players have to decide which creatures they rolled they will ready; they can only ready creatures they can pay for out of the currency rolled.  All creatures the player readied will now attack every other player; each player will defend the attack with their readied creatures; some will perish defending their player, and thus will not score.  Finally, the player may recruit one die from the common area with any remaining currency.  Play continues until one player accumulates the necessary glory to win the game!

Quarriors! has a higher luck quotient than its deck building cousins, and the game's combat mechanics are straightforwardly ruthless: you cannot play favorites, nor can you be merciful, you will attack everyone.  The greatest conscious tension in the game is deciding what to do with your rolled dice, as readying a creature will diminish your ability to recruit another creature.  Some creatures are cheap to ready, won't survive most attacks, and score few points, while other creatures cost a premium but will score a handful of points.  The strategy of the game lies in curating the dice in your bag as well as making the best of every situation.

Each game only sees a few sets of dice brought out to play with, the others saved for a future game.  Additionally, each set of dice has more than one card describing its abilities, so future games with the same dice will not always grant the same power, offering a wide array of replay options.  The game plays fast, with little in the way of down time and setup time.  It is heavier on luck than deck building games: you know what dice you will roll, so you know your chances, but you don't know what you will roll.  You may, or may not, get that creature you were hoping for.  This can be both a good and a bad thing, for it levels the playing field allowing a less skilled player to still have a chance to win the game, but a highly skilled player can still lose with a few bad throws of the dice.  Because of this the game should be played in accordance with the theme: have fun, take it lightly, enjoy yourself, and play for the social interaction.

RATING: N/A
Quarriors is like playing Dominion with a very watered down deck. Like whenever you gain a Kingdom card, you actually gain 3 copies of it, 2 Copper, and a Silver.

The fact that the only real decision you make, which die to add to your 'deck', is undermined by the fact that every time you actually draw that die it's only a 50% chance it'll do it's job is regrettable.

Dominion was ingenious and innovative, but still wasn't a very deep game. Quarriors is less deep and less interesting than Dominion without being any easier to teach, set up, or play.

The most unfortunate thing about Quarriors is because of the nice packaging (and despite the shoddy dice who's info rubs off almost immediately) and the pretty dice, and the fact that it's the first kid on the block to put a new twist on Deck building... it's going to sell a ton and be very popular. Any actual game using the "dice building' mechanism, no matter how much better than Quarriors, will suffer [i]precisely because[/i] it's not as simple or quick as Quarriors.

RATING: 7
This one is just as good as I expected.  Adds the fun of dice with the deck-building mechanic.  It's fun to roll the dice and see what could have been and what you ended up with.  I'm a big fan of games that force you to adapt your strategy on the fly.  Another big plus of this one is the speed.  I'd say it's over too quickly in the 4p game.  Three players definitely seems to be the sweet spot.  I can see the gripes about the writing on the dice being too small or the bags being too tight, but those are minor issues for this super-fun game.  My only personal issue is that with 4 the game seems too short.

After sitting back and thinking about this, I'd have to say that the idea of this game is sound, but the implementation might not come off in a way that gamers will like.  I like randomness in games when it causes you to adapt your plan to overcome it.  This game seems like sheer randomness is all you get.  Downgraded to a 7 and on the For Trade list.

RATING: 8.5

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 4
I played a 4P game with the Rise of the Demons expansion and was a little underwhelmed by it without really knowing why. That game featured a few cards that couldn't be used to their full effect and so there weren't many cards that could be bought. 

A year and a half later, I revisited the base game in two 3P games and felt even more underwhelmed by it. In most deckbuilders, you can get nerfed by a bad draw. In Quarriors!, you can get nerfed by a bad draw [i]and[/i] a bad roll, which makes the game too random for me to appreciate. You don't get a lot of options either - you roll your dice, and then you decide what you can do with your roll. Those choices are often limited, because you'd just want to summon any creatures rolled and buy something with any leftover money. The combination of the lack of decision space, coupled with the continuous drawing and rolling of dice gets repetitive quickly, even within the first game.

The components look lovely (good luck squinting at the dice though) and the mechanism of dice replacing cards in a deckbuilder is clever. It's a shame that the rest of the game doesn't live up to the premise.

I like how the use of cards + dice makes it easy to expand/change the dice available. Not 100% sold on the victory condition -- in my only play, it felt like a race to get a dragon out for scoring, and the rest of the dice you bought really didn't matter too much.

I'd like to play more, but don't see myself ever owning this.

Update: played this twice more and it felt a lot better, so maybe my first play was a little flat with the dragon and promos in it. It was pretty fun and I could see myself owning this eventually.

UPDATE: heh, 40 plays and 2 expansions later, I'm still digging this. Good lunch game, too, the guys all like it. We did make up our own set of variants and printed them on cards, similar to what some other people have done. We draw 2 random rule changes at the start of each game.

RATING: 5
Heard and read a lot of negative things about this game from trusted gamers and geekbuddies, so I never sought out to try it. Plus most deck builders I've played are not exactly the most sublime design, so there were some biases going in.

What I found on first play was more strategy and tactics than I had heard there would be. But the game was kinda boring with 4 players. And adding an extra layer of luck onto deck builders is not something the genre needed, I think.

Perhaps consider it as a dice game, and not a deck-building game, fine. But it fails there because there is no excitement. The literal purpose of dice is to create excitement, and this game does not really do that. There's some small tension in hoping your opponents don't roll what they need to kill your monster, but it does not justify the hundreds of dice or whatever that this game comes with.

As a gamer's game, in the long-run, I think Puzzle Strike mops the floor with this when it's evaluated as a deck building game, and King of Tokyo devours it as a quick dice filler game.

Despite the lightness I wouldn't feel comfortable playing it with non-gamers or anything either because the dice can be hard to read, etc.

The game was more fun than I'd heard, though.

[b]I'd Rather Play:[/b] Puzzle Strike

(3 Design + 2 Enjoyment)

RATING: 2
When described, this game sounds perfect.  I love the idea of how the dice are used for different monster cards.  I love the special powers.  I love the use of the bag rather than shuffling cards.  The idea of direct interaction is refreshing. 

But man, this was miserable. The big selling factor in the game is that it is supposed to be a quick filler.  But after playing an hour and 45 minute three player game, where no one could make any traction to finish the damn thing, I got to see how there aren't really any real choices to be had.  It was awesome to finish with zero points and the other players saying how nothing I could have done would have made any difference.

When you get behind the curve, your bag is filled with crap dice, this means that you aren't likely to get a couple monsters out to have one or two of them able to soak up hits.  Instead, you can just throw your monster to the wolves and repeat this over and over again.

All the decision with what to do with your dice are pretty straight forward, so the majority of your "decision making" is to roll well.  If you keep rolling ones, you just aren't as good at this game as those that are able to roll a couple monsters and high money.

Fall 2013

RATING: 6
I honestly don't know what to think of this one. There are some truly brilliant ideas going on within the game, and the production value is absolutely top notch. That said, I'm not sure whether the game is just a chase to catch up with the leader at this point... I would like to play it multiplayer before I form a firm opinion on the subject (we played three two player games).

Upon further reflection, I think that the main issue with the game is timing. The powers are simply too powerful for the brief period of time played if using the "automatic victory point" levels. For example, in a three player game, reaching fifteen points could very well take ten minutes or less if the right players get the right dice in combination.

RATING: 7
Nice variation on the deck-building theme, and sufficiently solves the problem endemic to deck-builders--when equally skilled players play, the luckiest shuffle wins (there is zero skill involved, literally).  There is additional luck inserted into the system at the dice roll part--with the expert rules from the expansion--this luck inserts tactical decisions into the system.  Yes, it is possible to have repeated sub-optimal rolls, but a game-long string of them is highly unlikely.

RATING: 5
I see the similarities with deckbuilding card games and appreciate an attempt to shake up that model. However, this game just doesn't seem to work. Way too easy for a lucky roll or two to make one player untouchable and give him/her a runaway victory.

True example of a 4-player game: Player 1 has a dragon out. Player 2 rolls his dice. Player 1 declares: "Player 2, your dragon came up as a tear drop (money), so you can't touch my dragon. Player 3, your dragon's in the discard pile and you have dice in your bag so you can't roll it on your turn. Player 4, you don't have anything that can beat my dragon. So I win!" Phooey. That wouldn't be so bad if it didn't happen on like turn 6, where we had barely played anything.

Still, many people seem to love it because it's fast and hey, lots of dice! To each their own. I'd play it in the right situation, mostly to be polite and sociable, but I'm not going to suggest it.

RATING: 8

The dice are beautiful. A few of mine seemed to have difficulty in printing and you have to squint to see what you just rolled, but that's not a game breaker at all. The cards are very well laid out with fun art. The game has a limit to spells and monsters per game, which makes it easy to create a plan and see where your opponents may be going. Ultimately; tons and tons of fun if you can get down with the random.

RATING: 7
First, what a great idea! Second, I love dice games.  This is a dice bag builder (not a deck builder).  No tedious shuffling.  Fast because it's dice.

Now, it's complicated at first.  Once you have the mechanics down it is much easier and faster to play.  I found a player "mat" online that I printed out and it was perfect for us beginners.  Note, when your dice bag is empty, add all the used dice back into the bag and finish drawing up to 6 dice during the draw phase.  All 10 pages in the instructions and it's in one little spot that I missed, (FYI).

RATING: 8
Dominion invented the genre of deckbuilding and many others have taken the idea and twisted it.  I've played almost all of them, but none of them are as much fun as Quarriors.  This game is pure fun.  There's not a lot of meaningful decisions, but there are lots of decisions.  It seems like a lot of the dice are about the same power level which means that they'll all be generally useful.  This avoids one of my big problems with Dominion.

I'm really looking forward to more plays of Quarriors.  I've started my rating at 7, but I think this will go up a lot.  I'm trying to be cautious with this one because I love the fun factor.  I don't want to set myself up for failure with this one.

Purchased from CSI 8/16/11

RATING: 7.5
Quarriors at face value is a very fun game.  It is super simple and super quick...perhaps too much so.  I found there to be little dice "building" in this dice building game.  You just grab what you can and roll.  Unlike other deck building games, where you are working towards creating a finely tuned deck.  Quarriors is over before things get started.

This doesn't mean it isn't fun.  Rolling the dice is fun!  I just think it needs some house rules to be better :)  And house ruling this game should be super easy.

Update 9/18/2011: After playing a couple games with additional rules, I think this game earned it's rating I've giving.  It could be a potential 8 with the changes I played with.  Two rules we used are: Play to 20 points and play with 1 of each spell and creature.  We also used a modified "assistant" variant rule found on this forum which I enjoyed a lot.

Kockás dominion, ahol "laphúzás" után még meg is kell dobni azt, amit szeretnél használni. Ráadásul a pontszerzésre a köröd végén kint hagyott kocka-kártyádat minden ellenfeled megtámadja egyszer a saját körében és csak akkor kapsz érte pontot, ha életben marad.

A többiek szemmel láthatóan élvezték a játékot, de nekem a szürnyes-trollos téma miatt kimondottan taszító volt. Ráadásul ezt a Gosus-franciás grafikát kimondottan utálom.

Szóval nagyon nem tetszett a játék, mert minden olyan dolog benne van, amit én nem kedvelek egy játékban. És hiába a Dominion-os alap, a Thunderstone-al ellentétben ez már nekem túl van az ingerküszöbömön.

RATING: 5
While somewhat hyped as Dominion with dice, I thought Q missed the mark in several respects. First, the game concept is not the simple, "Take an action and buy something," while the game doesn't have the depth of "engine" construction of Dominion. (For example, in a Dominion game just after Q, I used doubled King's Courts to activate Envoys to draw nearly my entire deck, not only letting me access additional actions, buys and treasures, but also powering Horn of Plenty.) Q is just set the warriors up and see if they survive, with complexity but not depth. The dice make the warriors unreliable, sometimes just turning into money. The explanation cards for the different dice can be hard to see (as are some dice faces), and they're far more complex than Dominion cards, even though they end up providing a rather simplistic experience.

RATING: 4.4
Quarriors is interesting for its bag-building mechanic, but I think that a game with this much direct player interaction ought to have more determinism. It also seems that regardless of the spread of available dice, only a few types ever get purchased. (This is exacerbated by the rather low supply of dice of each type; if your opponent is buying anything remotely good you better get in on it before all five? of them are gone!)

I think Quarriors probably got stronger when it got reimplemented as Marvel/D&D Dice Masters, but those incarnations have their own problems of being collect-em-alls on top of the fact that the game mechanics here just aren't that interesting to me.

This doesn't make it un-fun, but it does make a good portion of the options a waste.

RATING: 7
Quarriors has received some very mixed reviews, but I have to give the designers credit ... a deck-building game with dice is a very cool idea. Admittedly, though, I am a sucker for anything with specialty dice. As for the game itself, I want to like it more than I do. The decisions aren't really challenging - buy what you can afford - and there are definitely balance issues, including the much-debated start player/culling advantage. I have at least some home that future expansions will bring more options and balance to the game. For now, though, it's a pleasant way to while away the time - I definitely like rolling dice better than shuffling cards!

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
Quarriors is a fun take on the deckbuilder, but the original Quarriors is poorly balanced and usually leaves us feeling unsatisfied. In Dominion, you can pick a strategy and combine cards in interesting ways. In Quarriors, due to all the random dice rolls, the short game length, and the mechanics that frankly just needed more playtesting, it's just really unlikely to ever get interesting combos. In practice you just always purchase the biggest monster you can, and hope to roll it. Runaway victories are common.

Official variants and house rules try to add more depth to the game, but these all have downsides since the game wasn't balanced around them, and at some point you have to realize the fundamentals just aren't there.

Also, the dice are small and hard to read.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
See the "advanced rules" in the variants section. 

http://www.boardgamegeek.com/thread/711697/dr-houserule-or-how-i-made-some-minor-changes-to

It changes Quarriors from a light, quick, filler, with heavy amounts of luck, random set up, and fun dice chuckin to a light, quick, filler, with heavy amounts of luck, random set up, fun dice chucking AND more meaningful decisions.  Truth be told, if you didn't like Quarriors this variant won't change it, but if you thought it was okay with potential this may fix it for you.

My rating and my games played from my 5th play on reflect it in that fashion.

From game 9 on, we have played to 25 for 2 player games.  Again, its an easy recommend, as it help creates the dice building feeling that is somewhat lost with the score = cull variant I prefer.

The game as published is a 6 or 7.

RATING: 9
Quarriors! is a very fun game and is quite similar in game play to Dominion and Nightfall.  

You roll your dice and use the result to either put out creatures or buy another die.  The choices you make are not arbitrary, and every bit as interesting as they are in Dominion, except for the fact that Quarriors does not have the combo synergy yet that Dominion has.

I like having the creatures in play and the special abilities that come up if you roll stars on the die.  I like the fact that your creatures automatically attack each player when they come into play.  No one feels "picked on" like you do when you play Nightfall.  The downside is that there are fewer attacking choices with creatures.  But you can single out a player's creatures for individual smack down by using the spell dice.  Some of them,like the purple one, let you kill any one creature in play if certain conditions are met.

People who whine about this game "playing itself" or being too luck oriented probably just like to whine, or hate dice games.  If you hate dice games as a genre don't review this game.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

I like to have a FEW interesting decisions in a game.

RATING: 8

RATING: 6
I really want to like this game more than I do.  I love dice and the idea of a dice building just makes me happy on the inside, but this games one major drawback is there is so little player interaction.

I love buying dice, I love playing dice, and I love drawing dice, but once your turn is over you're basically sitting there waiting for your turn again.  Things do happen to you but there's so little that you can actually do about it.

The game also has the problem with having a really slow start and then finishing before you even realize it.  The slow start comes from every player having the same 12 starting dice so they pretty much have the same first 2 turns.  Nothing really interesting happens, people just buy dice and then pass.  There are many house rules to fix this problem, my group usually gives so many points that so that players can buy dice before the game starts.

Just when the game starts to get interesting, the game ends (unless you're playing 4 players).  If you're playing with less than 4 players, the game ends at a really low number of points and we usually just play to 20 as if we were playing a 4 player game.

Despite the complaints, I like this game.  It's not something I want to bring out all the time but just the idea of picking and rolling dice is fun, even if waiting for your turn isn't fun.  It's not something I would recommend to everybody or even tell someone to rush out to buy, but it's a fun game.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6.3
A fun little game. Has some gameplay issues - most noticeable is a slippery slope with the cull mechanic where once you score, your dice get more efficient and you end up getting quite a significant advantage. However, the dice rolls can be so random that this is kind of overshadowed. There's strategy here, but it's coated in a thick layer of randomization, so be warned: don't take this one too seriously!

Some cards are pretty overpowered and while it's nice that this makes some layouts have a certain strategy to them, it also kind of makes it obvious and boring (most noticeable are the Mighty Defender of the Pale and some Quake Dragon/Growth combos). Spells aside from Growth in general seem underwhelming and bland and are rarely bought.

Dice quality is rather bad (the numbers are near unreadable on some die), but I guess this is fixed in expansions, and will also be fixed in a new version of the base set to come, so that's great!

Pro-tip for dice storage: don't use the little baggies they come in, just toss them in one big bag. It may make less sense at the time but it ends up being so much faster and easier to set this game up than fiddling with lots of baggies.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 8.5
PERMANENT PLACE IN COLLECTION

UPDATED: With Quartifacts and the advanced culling rules, Quarriors is now a proper strategic deck builder with opportunities for re-rolling, attack, defense, boosts, etc. It's obviously still luck-based and has the possibility for unlucky rolls and mega-turns, but I don't mind that. It's genuinely very very fun with two.

UPDATED: Rating keeps creeping up. Quarriors ain't Dominion. It's more like King of Tokyo or Zombie Dice. It's random and very very silly. However, it's become our 'to-go' daft dice game and gets played a lot despite being objectively not very good. The McDonalds of games, perhaps. 

UPDATE: Upgraded from 6.5 to 8 with the new Quarmageddon rules. A quick light game with oodles of custom dice and cartoon monsters. What's there not to like...

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
Deck-building meets dice?  Sign me up!  It's about what you would expect from something described that way (you get an additional vector of randomness due to the roll of the dice you draw), but the same principles that apply to other deck-building games -- streamlining, building up currency before turning to points, having dice that interact well with each other -- apply here, and so long as folks enjoy playing with dice, I don't think they'll have a problem with the game.  I can see some potential issues, but they're not enough to turn me away from the novelty of the game.

UPDATE: I'm only two plays in, but so far, I'm not sure that this game has a lasting appeal.  The attrition rate of the game seems very slow for a deck-builder, partly because you don't ever have a guarantee of getting the ride side of the die you buy.  It's bad enough that you only see certain dice a few times through a "deck," but when you don't even get to use it for why you bought it, it's frustrating, and guaranteed to ensure a loss.  I'm not quite ready to give up on it, but it's starting to lose some of its shine, even at such a small number of plays.

1 play: 2 players
1 play: 3 players

RATING: N/A
In Quarriors, each player plays as a Quarrior competing for Glory in the eyes of Quiana, the Empress of Quaridia. Each player starts with an identical set of 12 dice in their dice bag. At the start of the game, Creature and Spell dice (Quarry) are dealt at random to form "the Wilds" in the center of the table. As play progresses, players roll their dice to attempt to summon Creatures, cast Spells, and harness the magical power of Quiddity (the in-game resource) to capture Quarry from the Wilds to add to their repertoire and into their dice bag. Players draw and roll 6 dice a turn from their bag, making that controlling bag composition is key to victory.
\13\\13\
Quarriors is a fast-paced game where players must strategically balance their choices each turn. Do I use my Quiddity to summon Creatures in the hopes of scoring Glory or should I spend it all to capture more powerful Quarry from the Wilds? Players must outmaneuver their opponent's through strategic Spell use, the acquisition of powerful Quarry, and ultimately, by striking down opponent's Creature in combat. If your Creatures survive until your next turn, you will score Glory points and move closer to victory!

RATING: 6
Quarriors! has completely ambushed me. I demoed it at Gen Con and came away mostly ambivalent about it. I enjoyed my game, but I didn't feel like it had enough to offer as a strategy game. Fast forward a few months later. I found an abandoned pre-order copy at the local FLGS being offered for less than even online pricing. I went ahead and picked it up on a whim, thinking that I didn't hate my one play at Gen Con and that the kids might like it...and do they ever! The game has been a hit, even with the kids that aren't really into gaming. The X-factor here seems to be the combat system, which generally speaking, doesn't allow one player to pick on another. The whole table has to take the hit from every creature that's played. That this even matters might seem silly, but trust me, that little design decision makes for an incredibly peaceful family game session.

As you've probably read a hundred times by now, there is a ton of luck and a runaway leader problem. (Probably due to that non-targeted aggression.) The game plays so fast though, that it doesn't really make sitting through a bad game all that painful. There are a few variants from the designer floating around to address some of the complaints, but I'm not really sold on them. Culling only the dice that score makes the dice bag feel stagnant and removes the feel of "deck building". While buying multiple dice per turn makes the game end too quickly via the four empty pile rule. I think maybe the best fix is to just raise the Glory Point target. That would give everyone's dice bag time to develop and maybe some of these problems might take care of themselves...

RATING: 7
Quarriors! is a surprisingly entertaining dice game that almost reminds me of Magic: the Gathering in some ways. Like Magic, I think Quarriors! is at its best and most strategic with 2 players and more than that just tends to devolve the experience into chaotic tug-of-war.

I like the variety of spells and creatures, which means games will often be different based on the combination of dice available for purchase. It is a DICE game, so complaining about an element of randomness is a bit silly. Again, the game feels like it manages this randomness best in a 2-player game, where watching your opponent's choices and developing a counter-strategy is crucial and will win you games. With 3 to 4 players, I can agree that the chaos will tend to take over.

I enjoyed Quarriors! quite a bit and certainly recommend it to people who aren't afraid of rolling dice. The production quality is great and the gameplay is good fun.
